Introduction

 

A

s I look at family pictures of Mom and Dad and us as kids, more than seeing just a nostalgic picture, I look into their eyes and try to imagine their lives from their perspectives at those times.

Mom and Dad were simply a man and woman raising a family and running a business, with all the concerns for family and business issues that everyone experiences, not certain that any of the success that eventually came would come.

To me, of course, Mom was Mom, Dad was Dad. What they did is what they did. And what we had or didnt have was just whatever happened to be. I never gave any thought to the individual beliefs and choices they made and how it impacted me. Fortunately, they were successful and raising a family and running a business that provided me with everything I needed and could have wanted. In the midst of it, I took a lot of it for granted until I began to realize what it took for me to achieve whatever it was that I achieved.

Then it sparked an interest in me to ask questions. At the time, I didnt have the insight to ask the questions or perhaps even process the answers. But overtime and contemplation, I have grown in my interest and ability to do that.

In the last few years, as I have contemplated many, many things, I have come to believe that everything we are and do and have is a result of our beliefs. At the core, it is a greatly simplifying concept. However, the way it plays out in each of us is highly complex.

I deal with regrets in this work, but at the top of the list is that I did not know this fundamental concept from the time I was old enough to understand it. It would have put so many things into a perspective that I could have effectively dealt with them, from my own personal identity, to every relationship I have ever had.

For years, I have been imagining how I could get my old photographs out of nested file folders and my experiences out of my mind into a form that provides this insight to anyone that may be interested in an accessible and hopefully interesting way, whatever the level of interest.

I wanted it to offer depth where someone may want it, but summary when not. I wanted it to provide a perspective of what it has been like to be me for whatever insight that may provide, good or bad.

I wanted it to reveal the unplanned and imperfect experiences shaping my beliefs, values, vision and behavior--and the life I have lived, but in a way that may be useful to those following their path behind me.

Its easy to look back at what was and not even consider that there were alternative outcomes to whatever actually happened, and were the result of the decisions made in times of uncertainty with the best beliefs one has managed to assemble at that point in time.

This is the personal legacy if I can use such a pretentious word that I want to leave my children and their children for whatever benefit it may give themwhether just to document memories or to provide some beneficial insight as what to do or not to do.

This digital book, in the format of a journal, asking and answering questions and providing little tangents into mini albums made up of the photos and videos I have collected.

 

Early in life, our focus seems to naturally be on ourselves, which is probably right. We're trying to figure out who we are, how we fit in, and importantly, how we're going to support ourselves. But after a while, we start to realize that this experience will eventually end, and we start to contemplate this experience and how we might fit into the lives of your family and friends and those that will come after us.

We are our beliefs. Our identities are shaped by our experiences that inform our beliefs about the external domains of the world and the internal domains of ourselves. Everything we experience informs our beliefs about ourselves and others. Often, it is not intentional, but rather haphazard. While preparing Mental Edge, I became aware of the importance of beliefs. As I prepared this project, I became aware of the importance of experience in shaping them, and how, while we cannot script our lives exactly, there are things we can do to guide the experiences that inform beliefs in ways that serve us. We knowingly try to teach our children, but I think, at least for me, we unknowingly and unintentionally inform beliefs, because there is no time when the mind is not experiencing and informing or shaping beliefs.

All we can really leave are memories in other people of the experiences of the senses and emotions that we helped foster in them. Although, I hope it is a long time from now, things for me will come to an end. My father's illness and end has caused me to reflect on his life and mine in new ways. And for better or worse, I have started thinking more about what my life has been about.

I am in the process of trying to come to an answer that for myself, which I suppose is the best one can do. There is no absolute truth about this. Whatever evidence there may be is circumstantial and based on applying some reason and 'faith' of some kind to fill in the gaps.

In a conversation with Elizabeth recently, I used the analogy that there is no paved road from unknowing to knowing. But rather, the gathering of 'evidence' takes the form of steppingstones between which small 'leaps of faith' based on some probability of truth help guide the way to an answer--at least for oneself.

I'll probably summarize what I think on this issue in what I plan to be preparing now, but whatever the 'answer' or 'conclusion' I come to, there is a current reality that I am part of, and having crossed at least the halfway point, whether I have done any of it before, now seems to be a good time to take a more active role in reflecting on and making the most of this life.

As I have reflected on my father's life, while I, of course, know a lot about what I experienced as part of his life, I find myself wanting to understand more about his life--his private, personal life--how he has seen the world and the relationships and experiences he had. What influenced him to think and be the person he is. At this point, he is unlikely to be able to offer that, even though, because I know from what he has already provided me and the sweet soul that he is, if asked, he would do whatever he could. What I would like for this 'work' to be is that for me to my family and those that may be interested in whatever benefit that may be to them. Again, whatever the answer to the afterlife, it seems the right thing to do to offer to those that come after some 'light' on their potential paths and the potential help of at least knowing the experience (I would not call it advice) of those that have travelled similar paths.

I am thinking about writing about my memories of the past and even speaking to my children into the expected future events of their futures whether I am here or not.

All we can really leave are memories of the experiences of the senses and emotions that we help foster in them. Although I hope it is a long time from now, Things for me will come to an end. My fathers illness and approaching end has caused me to reflect on his life and mine in new ways. And for better or worse, I have started thinking more about what my life has been about.

These stories aren't just about what happened - they're about what I learned along the way. They're about the challenges faced, mistakes made, lessons learned, and wisdom gained. Some stories will make you laugh, others might make you think, and hopefully, all will help you understand both your heritage and your potential.

The purpose of this book is to communicate the stories of my life to teach and entertain. It has been both for me assembling it, and I hope if is useful in some way. This book is divided into 2 main sections. The first is a series of stories in relatively chronological order as they happened during my life. The second is a set of stories about specific themes that have been important or may be of interest in my life.

Read these pages not just as history, but as a potential map. Whether you're my child remembering shared moments, or my grandchild discovering your family's story, these pages hold more than memories - they hold what I hope are insights that might help guide your own path.

 

Legacy Thoughts

I want my children to read and appreciate my life as one they can understand and empathize with - the challenges, the uncertainties. My success wasn't guaranteed. It came through hard work and sometimes difficult decisions.

I want my grandchildren to see these same experiences from an earlier perspective. The challenges they face now, that seem so life-altering, I experienced too. Despite uncertainties, I managed to build a successful life. Their challenges are not insurmountable.

 

My Birth


When were you born?

I was born on August 31, 1959, at 3:48 am. Dad was 24 and Mom was 20.

 

 

 

 


Where were you born?

I was born in Johnston Willis Hospital, Henrico County, Richmond, Virginia. where my dad had been stationed in the Navy and was working for Moffat Bearings as a salesman.

Whats the story behind your name?

I was named Michael Alan Bond simply because they liked the way the names sounded together. There was no one they were naming me after.

Because liked it, they decided to call me by my middle name, which led to a lifetime of explaining. Teachers would always start the year calling me Michael, and I'd have to correct them. Later, it became a handy way to screen phone calls - if someone asks for Michael, I know they don't really know me.

Like James?

Having the last name Bond has also added name challenges. The question is always, Bond? Like James? Only a few realize ahead of time that we must get that all the time. I even got the question in my Latin America travels, Bond? Cmo James?

What was happening in the world when you were born?

The world was a busy place politically, culturally, scientifically, and in business in 1959.

How much did things cost in 1959?

Its almost shocking to see how much things cost in 1959 versus today.

         Loaf of bread: $0.21

         Gallon of milk: $1.00

         Cup of coffee: $0.25

         Dozen eggs: $0.57

         New home: $12,400

         New car: $2,200

         Gallon of gas: $0.25

         Movie ticket: $1.00

         Pair of shoes: $4.00

         Plane ticket: $100

         Pizza: $1.00

         Beer/Liquor/Wine: $0.22/ $0.75/$0.50

         Newspaper: $0.05/Magazine: $0.25

It is also shocking that the average salary was $5,300.

 

In 1959, they had only recently invented cash registers to total groceries and calculate change. Before that people actually had to know how to do math.

 

Family Names

 

What is the story behind the family names?

The Bond name is from England and the Davis name is from Wales

What do you know about the family origins?

I have never investigated our family origins beyond my great grandparents. My aunt, Nell Hammond, created a list that I have, but I dont know how it would fit in with ancestry.com to find more.

I don't know if this is true, but I heard my Davis grandparents saying that someone in their family had traced their roots back to the Mayflower. Because my paternal grandfather left the family when my father was young, we don't know much about his history. I would one day like to use ancestry.com to trace our history.

The Bond Side

Bond is one of the many names that the Normans brought with them when they conquered England in 1066. It is a name for a husbandman or farmer.

The first recorded spelling of the family name is shown to be that of Norman le Bonde, which was dated 1180, in the "Pipe Rolls of Warwickshire", during the reign of King Henry 11, known as "The Builder of Churches", 1154 - 1189.

The surname is first recorded in the latter half of the 12th Century (see below), whilst other early examples include William Bonde of Warwickshire noted in the Records of the Knights Templar of England in 1185. Later examples of the surname recording include the marriage of Elianor Bond and Richard Laplove on November 12th 1576 at the church of St. Gregorys by St. Pauls in the city of London, and that of John Bonder, who married Elizabeth Webb at Knightsbridge, Westminster, on June 4th 1650.

Immigrants named Bond arrived in the early 17th century.

The Bond Motto

One of the early Bond mottos was "Orbis non sufficit" ("The world is not enough"), which is the story behind the James Bond movie of the same name. In one sense, this could mean possessing the world is not enough. But a more constructive meaning is that the world is not enough to contain our ambition and exploration.

The Davis Side

The Welsh Davis surname is patronymic, meaning son of David. Ultimately derived from the Hebrew name David, meaning beloved. The name became a popular given name throughout Medieval Europe due to the biblical king David of Israel. The popularity of the name was further increased in Britain due to it being the name of the Patron Saint of Wales. Little is known about Saint David, but he is thought to have been a 6th century monk and bishop. The name came to be used as a patronymic name by the Brythonic people of Wales.

One of the most famous bearers of this personal name in Wales was David ap Gryffydd, the last Prince of North Wales, who was executed c. 1276 by King Edward I of England.

In the early 1500s, Welsh and English families with Davis and similar last names began to move to Ireland.

The first Irish-born Davys to achieve prominence in the country was Sir Paul Davys (C.1600-1672). He was born in Kill, County Kildare, and with the support of his lifelong friend, James Butler, 1st Duke of Ormonde, became Ireland's Secretary of State. His career spanned many governments, and he had considerable influence over Irish affairs for more than 40 years. His son, Sir William Davys (bef. 1633- 1687) became Lord Chief Justice of Ireland.

Larger groups of Davys, Davies, and Davis families moved to Ireland during the 1600s. Many settled in the counties of Cork, Galway, and Wexford.

The Davis Motto

Two Davis family mottos have been recorded:

It belongs to the Davis Baronets of Hollywood.

 

My Parents

 

T

he simplest and best way to describe my parents is, they loved each other, and they loved us. This simple truth was the foundation of every part of our family. They were willing and wanted to do anything and everything possible to support each other and to support us. They came from humble beginnings, instilled in us humble truths, supported our interests; and gave us opportunities and experiences that helped us grow.

My Father


 



When was your father born?

My father was born, Robert Warren Bond, November 8, 1934,

Where was your father born?

He was born in Greenwood, Mississippi, but they soon moved to Ita Beena, Mississippi, a very small town that had only one traffic light. .

Did your father have any siblings?

Yes, he had a sister, Barbara Nell Bond (link to Nell album), that was two years older.

What was he like when he was younger?

His early life was difficult, because his father left when he was about seven years old, leaving him, his sister and his mother to manage on their own. They all had to work various jobs to provide for the family. His mother, Margaret Bond, worked in a sewing factory, sewing cuffs and collars on shirts for 5 cents per item. Dad had two jobs: bagging groceries at a grocery store and delivering newspapers on a paper route.

What did your father do for a living?

Dad ran a business manufacturing and selling machinery for the textile industry. When he got out of the Navy, he worked for a power transmission company called Moffatt Bearing. After a year or so, he started a business with a friend of his for about $1,500. Over time he built it to over $10 million in revenue and won the Export Award for the state of South Carolina in 1977. He sold the business in 1983, bought it back and then sold it again.

After that, he started several other businesses, an employment agency, a screen printing business and a couple of textile related businesses. He was always thinking of ideas for businesses.

What beliefs or values did he instill in you?

The other broad thing I learned from him was how to discipline, but also how to love my children. There is no doubt he loved his children. He did a lot of things in his life, but his kids were always a tope priority. I recently found some postcards that he had written from Europe individually to each of us, individually encouraging us, and in hindsight in the way that would uniquely apply to us individually. In everything we did, he was always looking for ways to facilitate what was good for us.

When I was in the fifth grade, the school put on a program of orchestra instruments trying to encourage and interest students in taking up one of the musical instruments. When I got home I asked my mother it I could play the violin. She said Id have to ask my father. I was anxious all day as to what he would say. When he got home, I told him about the program and asked about playing the violin. Without hesitation, he said yes. Needed not other details. He was like that about everything when it came to us. I always looked and still look for ways I can help my children

Newspaper Delivery

For those who have only known the internet, people used to get a newspaper delivered to their house every morning. They would hire kids like Dad to collect a stack of newspapers, roll them up with a rubber band, ride their bikes from house to house throwing them onto customers driveways and porches. The job also included collecting money from customers and delivering it to his manager at the newspaper. Part of his discipline came from this job where he was required to straighten and arrange all of the bills (people paid in cash) in order from the largest bills in the back to the smallest in front, all face-front and smoothed of creases and wrinkles. If it were not done right, he would have to correct it before he got paid.

Slingshot

As a boy he carried a slingshot in his back pocket, using it to hit various objects, which unfortunately included unsuspecting birds. He wasnt a particularly good student in school, and, while he likely secretly envied his classmates going off to college, he boasted to them that by the time they got out of college, he would already be well on his way with his career.

Heart of Gold

Despite his challenging upbringing, my father had a heart of gold. I have never known anyone who cared more about other people and made sure they felt happy, included and important. He would get to know my friends and my brothers friends and talk to them when they came over to our house.

Vacations

When we were growing up, he made sure that we took great vacations together, all over the country to expand our horizons. We travelled to Texas and Atlanta to see relatives. We went to Washington, DC, New York City for the Bicentennial. He rented a Winnebago for a trip to Florida. I wont forget him trying to navigate the tiny streets of St. Augustine in that huge thing. We took what we called our Western trip, flying to Denver, taking a train to Ogden, Utah, ??, Montana, San Francisco, Las Vegas and the Grand Canyon.

No College

Even though he never went to college, he was hard-working and built a business that was good for his family, and he shared that success with everyone else at the company. Because he either couldnt but nevertheless didnt, he made sure that all of us were able to go to college. I was the first person in our family to graduate from college. All my brothers went and now my children and most of my brothers children have gone to college.

Navy Service

Luckily, my parents did not have to be involved in any wars. Dad was in the Navy. He was on an aircraft carrier, the USS Intrepid and helped launch the planes. He had some 8mm movie film of his time in the Navy that I had seen before, but it was lost in one of their moves somehow and I really wish we could have had it today.

First Priority

His first priority was providing for the family. He also loved his wife and his sons. He was so proud of all of us. Every time we would get together, he wanted to get the guys together and take a picture. Even when we were married and had families. He loved everyone, but he held the core of us in a special place.

Fear of Mice

One of Dads only fears that I knew of what his fear of mice because when he was little, he used to have to set and empty the rat traps under their house in Atlanta. He disliked them so much that he fired a man that found a dead mouse at the office and deliberately put it in his desk-side waste basket.

However, one weekend at the lake, my brother Gregs pet chipmunk jumped into the water As much as he disliked rodent-like animals, Dad dove into the water, swam back to get it, swam back to the boat and handed it back to Greg. He suppressed his fear or dislike to serve his son.

Saturday Morning Post Office

In the late 1960s, Dad had a mailbox at the main post office in downtown Greenville. On Saturday mornings, he would take us all to the post office with him to collect the mail. We would run around and play as he opened and sorted through the mail.

On one of our trips, he took us to Dairy Queen for ice cream. After getting the ice cream , he realized he was a few cents short and we had to search through the car seats to see if we could find enough.

No Role Model

When I consider my dad's life, the person he is, and the success he had, I am even more impressed that he had no father to learn from. He overcame not having a father and was very determined. He seemed to always know what he wanted. He was very determined in knowing he wanted to marry my eventual mother. He was determined and started a business when he was young, even without a degree, and he always made quick decisions based on how he felt about them, maybe even more so than the details or circumstances. I know how influential my father was in raising me, supporting me, disciplining me. To think that he did what he did without a father as a role model and support is even more impressive, and a compliment to his mother who had to be both mother and father.

As I have grown up, had a family of my own, been a part of business, a couple of the things I admire the most about Dad is that he was born very poor and his father left him very early in his life. However, his mother instilled good values in him that guided his entire life. Early in his life he spent a lot of time in church, and while I never heard why, he was not particularly religious, although he treated everyone as if he were.

What I admire most about my father is that despite being born very poor and losing his father early, he built a successful life guided by good values from his mother. He always seemed to know what he wanted and went after it decisively. Whether it was asking Mom out, starting his business, giving us opportunities or buying homes and property, when he saw what he wanted, he moved quickly.

What qualities did you get from your father?

I would like to think that I got several of my qualities from my father. From very early, my father taught all of us the "Big R"--Responsibility. Believe me, I hated learning it. When I was a teenager, I would cut the grass in front of his office building, and after I was through, I had to have him come inspect it before he would allow the accountant to pay me.. If I had missed any spots or hadn't raked up all the clippings, I would have to clean up those issues. It has served me well, though, because I learned to focus on taking care of my responsibilities, like my schoolwork or my job, first. But there are times when I wish I had not been so focused and enjoyed things a bit more. It's hard to say that, because who knows how things would have turned out if I had played more, but there was likely more room for play than I allows for, ensuring I met my responsibilities.

Care for Others

Another thing I learned from my father is to care for others. He always wanted to make sure everyone was included. Perhaps because he had not been when he was younger. He shared with us and he shared with the people that worked for him in the company. When I was about 9 or 10, I was the pitcher for my baseball team. I was a decent pitcher. In one of our games, we were winning by several runs at the end of the game, so the other team let one of their smaller players bat. I remember how small and scared he looked standing there holding the bat.

Trying to take it easy on him, I threw the first pitch really softly, missed the plate and the umpire called it a ball. My coach jumped off the bench, ran out of the dugout toward the mound yelling at me not to do that and to throw that ball in there and strike him out. It made me mad and sad at the same time. I got back on the mound and threw three straight fast balls as hard as I could right down the middle to end the game. The little kid started crying and I started crying. I felt so bad, but I have always had a heart for other people.

Dad never went to college. He never said this, but I assume it had to do with his inability to pay for it. But he told others he was going to get out and get into business. At that time, the country still had a draft where men 18 years old or older could be selected/drafted to join the military. His brother-in-law Charles (Toby) Hammond was already in the Navy. Dad loved and respected him. As protection against going into a branch that he didnt want to go into, he joined the Navy Reserves, and when/if he was drafted, he could just activate into the Navy. And that is what happened. Dad was sent to Richmond Virginia and served on the USS Intrepid.

What do you admire most about your father?

Success From Humble Beginnings

What I admire most about my father is that despite being born poor and losing his father early, he built a successful life guided by good values from his mother. Without a college education and only a couple of years of business experience, he started a business with about $1,500 that he built to provide livings for his family and hundreds of people over the years. His name and business became known favorably throughout the textile industry.

Making Decisions

Dad always seemed to know what he wanted and went after it decisively. He saw Mom at church and made arrangements to meet her, date her and ultimately marry her. Without a college education and only a couple of years of business experience, he started a business with about $1,500 that he built to provide a living for his family and a couple hundred people over the years. His name and business were known favorably through the textile industry until economics pushed the textile industry overseas. When we were spending many days at the YMCA in sports, and Mom was having to drive us 25-30 minutes each way, he found and bought a house right across the street from the Y so we could walk there for practice and games. When my brothers wanted to stay with their friends going to high school, he sold the house we had and bought another one in the right district. He was a thorough reader of the paper cover-to-cover. He would read everything looking for the most obscure items. One day he saw a 2-line ad for a piece of property on Lake Hartwell. He jumped in the car and drove 45 minutes with no other information to see it. He wrote a deposit check on the spot and it became a place for family and friends for yearsfirst a vacation home and later their permanent residence. He was never afraid to make a decision, and he always made decisions for the benefit of the family.

 

My father gave me the greatest gift anyone could give another person: He believed in me.

There once was a boy from Ita Bena.

I chose this title because it was something he said on time. He must have been considering his life, and while he was losing his ability to think, I hope it was something that gave him great pride. Because I am proud of him.

I was blessed with a father that had a heart of pure gold. Despite some circumstances that might have led him down a bad path, he managed to find and stay on one that would have him positively impacting the lives of hundreds, if not thousands of people. He was a gentlemen through and through, and a living example of the great character that it seems more and more we have lost in this world, and maintained it to the end. It was pure because it continued to pour out of him as he started losing the ability to think. He was always concerned about other people. It was manners, but it wasn't just manners. He had a true, heartful desire to help other people. At Lila Doyle, he would reach out to people who he felt were suffering. He made a friendship with Dr. ???, who was a Parkinson patient who had lost the ability to speak. Dad would push him up and down the hall. If Dad passed his doorway, even though he couldn't speak, Dr. ??? Would make loud noises in a clear attempt to get his attention. He would try to console others that were clearly upset. He would never begin eating without first ensuring that others were having something. I only hope that if I ever lose my capacity to control my thoughts that it is the purity of spirit that he instilled in me that comes out.

Dad was born in Ita Bena. By his own stories, he had a rough start. He told us about going around his neighborhood with a slingshot and shooting birds, and not being a particularly good student.

He moved to Atlanta when he was ???. He had a lifelong fear of mice and rats because, he had to set and empty the rat traps under his house in Atlanta. IN a rare display of anger, he fired an employee who, not realizing the depth of his dislike, thought it would be funny to put a dead mouse that had been caught in the office in his waste basket. His father left when he was ?? And never had the influence of a father, except by the example of one that had left. His mother and older sister must have been a tremendously strong influence on him. Even though he was the youngest, he always made sure he took care of his mother and sister. I remember how hard it was for him when she passed away. It was another example to me of how deeply and sincerely he loved his mother and how much he appreciated everything she had done for him. She had no education and he told me she used to work in a sewing factory, sewing collars and cuffs on shirts for 5 cents an hour.

He told us when he graduated from high school, he didnt want to go to college. He thought by the time others went to college for 4 years, he would already have a job and be making money. He changed his mind about this later and it became one of the things in life he missed the most. He took a few night classes at Georgia Tech, the school for which I think he had the utmost respect, but he never completed a degree. He joined the Navy, where his sister's husband, Toby was. He always had a great deal of respect for Toby.

Despite, or maybe because of, not having a degree, he was always driven. When he got out of the Navy, he got a job with Moffat Bearings selling power transmission equipment. But it wasn't long before he got together with a partner and $1,600 and started Bond Transmission & Controls, selling power transmission equipment. I remember being in the building. Mom worked as the receptionist/secretary.

I used to, and still do like to look at pictures of people when they were younger and look into their faces and eyes and think about what they must have been thinking about. Looking back at pictures of the family, you can look into his face and see the happiness and pride that his family gave him. He was married to a woman who he dearly loved, and he had four boys who where the center of his universe. Up until he was unable to do it, every time we got together as a family, he wanted to get a picture of just the boys. We have hundreds of pictures of me, then me and Scot, then me, Scot and Greg and then me, Scot, Greg and Mark. He didn't exclude others on purpose, he was just so happy and proud of his family. I am sure it is what helped give us the confidence and belief in ourselves to go out and lead lives of our own, with our own families and pass on the love that he always had and gave to us.

Tribute to My Father

This is the talk I gave at Dads funeral.

Good afternoon! My name is Alan Bond. I am Bob's favorite oldest son.

I want to thank you all so much for coming. We are honored that he touched you in a way that led you to want to be here.

I also want to thank everyone at Lila Doyle for the love and care you gave Dad and Mom over the last 5 tough years. You all are truly special people.

We are sad, but we are relieved he is no longer suffering. You saw the pictures outside. We want to celebrate the life he lived. I hope you'll bear with me a few minutes.

When we were growing up, we had a short little prayer that Dad liked on a plaque in our den that I think epitomized the way he lived his life. I am sure you've heard it. It's called the Serenity Prayer. It goes,

         Lord, grant me:

         The Serenity to accept the things I cannot change,

         The Courage to change the things I can, and

         The Wisdom to know the difference.

There were many things that he could not change.

His father left when he was very young, they didnt have a lot of money. His mother worked sewing cuffs and collars on shirts for 5 cents apiece. He worked in a grocery store and had a paper route. He had a lifelong fear of mice because he used to have to clear the rat traps under their house. He never got a degree--something he always regretted. He had scoliosis, which caused him tremendous back pain later in life, and of course he suffered with Parkinson's and dementia for the last 10 years.

But, he never complained.

I never heard him say anything negative about really anything. He always had a positive disposition, a love for other people

He had the Courage to change the things he could.

He seemed to have an intuition about what was right. When he saw it, he went after it.

I think he saw what was right in Mom.

They met at church. One night he came up behind her, tapped her on the shoulder and asked, "What's your name?" She turned away and didn't answer! He tapped again and asked, "What's your name?" She just said, "Sandra". He called later to ask her out, and she said, "My Daddy doesnt let me go out with people he doesnt know." Undeterred, somehow he found out there was a girl that he knew that knew her dad, and he said, so and so knows me, Have him ask about me. He did and approved and they were together from then on. Both had come from broken families, so when he asked her to marry him, she said, Yes, but only if you'll promise me 50 years. On September 14, 2018, they celebrated 61 years together. She said the other day, "He gave me the 50 years, with interest. "

On the business side, despite his lack of a degree, he and Bill Alexander started a business in 1967 with $1,200 dollars, which is about $8,000 today. He, with help from many, built that business over time to employ hundreds of people, and the name BOND is still recognized in the textile industry for high standards.

And, he always wanted to share his good fortune.

I remember as a young business student arguing with him when he wanted to give away equity in the company. He did it anyway and he was right. When he sold the business, he gifted some of the proceeds to every relative we had, and funded life scholarships at Clemson and South Carolina.

And, he always loved getting the family together.

When we were very small, he bought a house boat on lake Hartwell, and we spent many, many summer weekends on the lake either just us or with friends. It was a big part of our childhoods and he kept that boat for 40 years because of the memories it held for him.

I think those of you at Lila Doyle know that he read the paper religiously, cover to cover every day. One day, he saw an ad for a piece of property on the lake. He hopped in the car right away, and when he saw it he bought it on the spot as a place for the family to get together. We enjoyed it for 20 years and we brothers partially raised our kids there.

When it came to the "Wisdom to know the difference" part of the prayer, there was very little in the 'could not change' column for Mr. Bond.

Once he had an idea that he thought he could make work, he almost never took no for an answer. And if one door closed, he would find another door to open. He was determined.

Throughout the stages of his Parkinson's his heart and spirit continued to shine through till the end.

In the early stages, when he was hallucinating, he would see a small girl sitting in a chair in their den. While we were eating, he would say he felt bad for her and ask if she was going to eat with us. At Lila Doyle, he made quick friends with Dr. Lee, another patient who had severe Parkinson's and couldn't communicate. Dr. Lee would hold Dad's walker, and Dad would push him around the halls in his wheelchair playing like two little boys.

I know he had more he wanted to accomplish, he was always working on the next thing.

But at their last anniversary get together, he looked around the table, and even though he stumbled with his words, we could tell he was proud of the family he had built.

One last story and I'll wrap it up

I mentioned one of the things he always regretted was not having a college degree. When he would be in meetings and others were sharing where they had gone to school, he always felt left out and lesser. One of his goals was for his sons to be able to go to college and earn the degrees that he never had. Today, he has a wall in his home displaying the degrees of all four of usand the degrees of 7 of his grandchildren.. with more on the way.

Bob Bond lived the Serenity Prayer.

This little boy from Itta Bena, MS accepted the things he absolutely could not change, and he lived with courage to change the things he could to make the lives of his family and others better.

So, today, I am asking the Serenity Prayer.

I can't change the cycle of life, but I can pass his legacy of love on to my children and their children.

Dad, You lifted us all and for that you can be at peace. We love you.

Thank you.

 

My Mother

When was your mother born?

My mother was born, Sandra Jean Davis, February 5, 1939,

Where was your mother born?

She was born in Augusta, Georgia.

Did your mother have any siblings?

She was an only child.

What was she like when she was younger?

She was friendly but fairly quiet growing up. She was an only-child and her dad was very strict. When Mom and Dad were first dating, he asked her, "Don't you ever talk?" She was a very traditional wife and mother. Dad was the breadwinner and decision maker, although he rightly always included her in decisions. And she liked that just fine - he made decisions that kept her in mind.

While she was born in and spent some time in Augusta, they moved to Illinois for some time--I believe because her father was looking for work. At some point later they moved to Atlanta. Mom graduated from Brown High School in May 1957.

My mother was born a saint. She had the four of us in less than four years and did everything for us while Dad was working. She was always there. I dont ever remember her ever wanting anything for herself. Even now, when we go to her house to visit, she is always busy cooking or cleaning or getting something ready for everyone else. When my brothers and I were playing sports, she used to drive us many miles to drop us off; wait for us to finish and return. I never heard her complain about having to do anything for us.

There are so many things I'd like you to know about my mother. She was an only child, and she grew up with a kind strict father. As a child, she was friendly, but fairly quiet. When Mom and Dad were dating, on one of their dates, he asked her, "Don't you ever talk?" She was a very traditional wife and mother. Dad was the bread winner and decision maker. He included her in the decisions, but he was pretty much the final decision maker. And she liked that just fine. Mom was almost 100% dedicated to the kids and Dad. She had very few interests outside of us. When we were teenagers, she joined a bowling league and enjoyed that, and later I found out she was in a social sorority that has few events, but not many.

Dedication

Mom was almost 100% dedicated to the kids and Dad. She had very few interests outside of us. When we were teenagers, she joined a bowling league and enjoyed that, and later I found out she was in a social sorority that Inever knew about. Looking back now, I realize she rarely expressed wanting anything for herself - she was always serving the family, either as Dad's wife or as our mother. When my brothers and I were playing sports, she'd drive us many miles to drop us off, go home, then come back a few hours later to pick us up. I remember many times when she would pick me up in the dark. I never heard her complain about having to do anything for us.

What qualities did you get from your mother?

First, I look more like my mother than my father. But I would say I inherited her quiet nature. Early on I was very shy. I had friends, but I was not extremely outgoing. And, for better or worse, I can be by myself for extended periods and be perfectly happy. My mind is always running, and I can spend hours in thought and exploration.

What things do you admire the most about your mother?

The thing I admire most about my mother is her sense of self-less ness. I only remember a few times that she ever expressed wanting something. She was always serving the family. Either us as kids or as Dads wife.

Their Partnership

How did your parents meet?

My parents' story began in high school. They met at church but went to the same high school. Dad was a senior and Mom was a freshman.

What stories do you remember of them dating?

What Was Your Name Again?

Years later, Dad confessed something about their first date that he didn't tell Mom until very late in their marriage, and I only heard after he passed away. On either their first date or one of their first dates, they went to the Brown High School Graduation. When they ran into some of Dad's friends, he didn't introduce Mom to them. The reason? He couldn't remember her name! Mom didn't say anything at the time, but Dad carried that secret for years.

Dont You Ever Talk?

Apparently Mom was very quiet. On one of their dates, she was not saying anything and Dad asked her, Dont you ever talk?You can see her quiet nature in the early photos of her. She became more outgoing later as her confidence grew, but never to the point of being extroverted.

When did they get married?

Despite this rocky start, they married on September 12, 1957, and built a beautiful relationship and have been together since.

I believe they had a great, loving relationship until the day he died. I don't remember ever hearing them argue. They may have, and probably did, but it was rare and never had any impact on us. I love looking at their travel pictures - they're mostly of Mom because Dad wanted to take her picture everywhere they went. The ones of them together show them smiling and laughing and truly enjoying themselves. Mom was also the family historian. She always had a memory for dates and names and places. "Hey Sandra, when did this happen?" Exact date. "What was the name of so and so?" Correct name.

Mom was almost 100% dedicated to the kids and Dad. She had very few interests outside of us. Looking back now, I realize she rarely expressed wanting anything for herself - she was always serving the family, either as Dad's wife or as our mother. When my brothers and I were playing sports, she'd drive us many miles to drop us off, go home, then come back a few hours later to pick us up. I remember many times when she would pick me up in the dark. I never heard her complain about having to do anything for us.

I don't remember ever hearing them argue. They probably did, but it was rare and never impacted us. I love looking at their travel pictures - they're mostly of Mom because Dad wanted to take her picture everywhere they went. The ones of them together show them smiling and laughing and truly enjoying themselves.

Are you more like your father or your mother? In what ways?

I think I have characteristics of but Id say I am more like my father. Both are kind considerate people, and I think for the most part I am kind and considerate. I have my moments. I tend to see situations from a whats good for everyone perspective like Dad did. He always wanted to make sure other people were being properly recognized and respected. He went above and beyond to take care of his family, especially his mother. He was also very giving to others, including his employees. Sometimes to the point they took advantage of him. But even then, he still wanted to do things for them. When I was in college, he was running his business. He told me he was giving share ownership to the employees. This was before it was common. Being a young business student, I didnt agree with doing it. But in hindsight he was right to do it. Treating everyone fairly and being appreciative of what others have done for me and what Ive done for them. I can occasionally take this too far.

I think I am more like my father in terms of discipline and perseverance. They are both compassionate, and I think I have some of that, although I can hide it. I think I am more like my mother in terms of demeanor. I am probably too quiet

Were your parents strict or relaxed?

I would say they were strict initially, but once we learned and fell into line, they were more relaxed. I remember a few spankings and talks that were not pleasant. But having been a parent I can empathize.

Did you ever rebel?

I cannot remember any time where I openly rebelled against my parents. I did things that they didnt know I was doing! But I never actively rebelled against them. Maybe I should have in some way. Or maybe the discipline we grew up with was enough to let me know that that was not going to be tolerated. but for whatever reason, I never really rebelled.

I do remember some things I disagreed with Dad about. I suppose I thought I knew better than him, but he always wanted to share ownership of the business with the employees. He started with one partner, but over the years as others joined them, he knew they were making valuable contributions and he wanted them to share in that. I objected to him doing that. Of course, I didnt really have a say, which is good, because in hindsight I completely agree with him. It is one of the things I dislike about our flavor of capitalism. In my opinion, not enough of the value created is shared with all of the employees that helped create it. Some is, but not nearly enough.

I did not disagree with him on this point, although I was too inexperienced to really understand his point at the time. But he always said that we pay the most important people the least and it should be the opposite. He was referring to teachers, police, firemen, etc. At the time he even felt legislators should be paid more. And perhaps they should. Perhaps if we paid them more they would steal less. although somehow experience has led me to believe that is not true. But I certainly agree with his point

What was your relationship with your parents like when you were a child, teenager, or adult?

I believe that had a great loving relationship and did until the day he died. I dont remember ever hearing them argue. They may have and probably did, but it was rare and never had any impact on us. I love looking at the travel pictures they have. They are mostly of mom because dad wanted to take her picture everywhere they were, and some had to be taking the picture (him!). The ones of them together show them smiling and laughing and truly enjoying themselves.

What was an important lesson parents taught, lived by shared with kids?

My parents taught me to respect "my elders" and authority. This is generally a good thing, and I have taught it to my children. However, it can be taken too far, if it makes you not stand up for yourself and put what other people want ahead of what is in your best interest.

 

My Siblings

Did you have any siblings?

I am one of 4 boys, NO GIRLS! We used to joke that the only girls in the family were Mom and her dog Suki.

How far apart in age are you?

The four of us are three years and 10 months apart. There is less than 12 months between number 2 (Scot) and number 3 (Greg). My mother tells the story of going in for her first checkup after Scot was born, finding out she was pregnant with Greg and breaking down crying. I can only imagine.

What was it like being so close in age?

There is sibling rivalry, and there is sibling rivalry between 4 boys very close in age.

Brotherly Rivalry

There is sibling rivalry and then there is the sibling rivalry that such closeness in age of 4 boys brings, when accompanied by two parents, young themselves, and having come from 1 and zero sibling families, brings. I am sure there were times that we were not arguing, but it seems there was fairly constant antagonism. That I remember, we only physically fought a few times, I remember. Whether it was arguing over toys, who was going to get the first cereal toy from every box of cereal ever advertised on television (which we never got), who was going to sit in the front seat, which channel (of three) we were going to watch, whether someone was sitting too close to them or on their side of the car or ate the last cupcake. This list of things we could and would argue about is endless. Having now experienced kids and grandkids of my own, and their sibling rivalry, I'm amazed at how Mom and Dad managed us all, especially Mom, who somehow kept her composure while dealing with our constant competition. Like most though, I think we grew out of it eventually and we get along very well now. We go in and out of various degrees of closeness and we appreciate each other more now.

Injury Prone

Not only were we in constant rivalry, but we seemed to be frequently injured. Between Greg, Mark and I, we had over 100 stitches for various injuries and Scot had none. I dont remember Greg and Marks, but I had at least 20 myself from 5 different injuriestricycle down the stairs: forehead; throwing rocks: back of the head; brick under the eye, glass to the back of my leg, and right knee gouge on a sliding board. I think at least 12 of Gregs stitches were in his forehead. Scot didnt have stitches, but he suffered a punctured eardrum playing Martian with me falling on a Q-tip. I knocked out one of Scots teeth while we were sock sliding on the linoleum floor. Mark knocked his own tooth out when he jumped out of the tree house with an umbrella as a parachute--maybe or maybe not influenced by his oldest brother suggesting he would float softly down. When Mom came home from work, she came running around the house to find me crying, You knocked my babys tooth out! Other than stitches, I had a broken hand from football and burned my leg severely screwing around with gasoline. In another symbol of immense intelligence, I jumped off the roof of our house with a sheet as a parachute. It really hurt and Im lucky I didnt break a leg or worse.

What was it like being the oldest?

Im not the proudest of my role as the oldest. When we were younger, I certainly leveraged my position as the oldest and the greater experience as leverage against my younger brothers. That dissipated later into somewhat more helpful and leadership or example setting to the extent I could, But, I am a little embarrassed to say that in hindsight, I could have and should have been better. Its not an excuse, but rather an explanation, that I think it was a result of my insecuritiesas all of our negative behaviors tend to be.

How similar or different are you?

I suppose this is the case with all siblings, but it is interesting and fascinating how similar we are in some ways and how different we are in others. Born and raised by the same parents, we possess many of the caring qualities they shared with us, but each of us has very differing interests. Certainly, nature plays a big role, but I have also come to believe that our experiences are very important. The experiences themselves are important, but the birth order imparts its own unique set of experiences because of the experiences both the parents and other siblings have experienced. The first is dealing with new, inexperienced parents. The last is dealing with experienced parents. Each situation carries its own advantages and disadvantages.

What did you do together?

We played and fought. Wed ride out bikes all over the neighborhood, which seemed large at the time. There was a creek at the end of the road and we would often use rocks or bricks to build a dam and play in the water that pooled. We would catch crawdads.

How did your parents handle discipline?

Our parents were strict, but loving. I remember some early disciplines that would be frowned upon today.washing the mouth out with soap, belt on bare skin, I remember being laid over the bed and spanked with a belt. But all of those were early. After that, I dont remember much punishment. Perhaps we got better, but perhaps they changed their ways. Having now had kids myself, I cannot imagine having four boys so clos ein age getting into absolutely everything. They have endless curiosity and energy and no beliefs to slow their exploration into areas that are dangerous either to them or others.

Who took care of you during the day?

Mom stayed at home mostly. There was a time early in my dads business where she would work as a secretary and my grandmother (Dads mother) would take care of us. But I dont think that lasted a long time.

What family activities were common?

Our lives were pretty simple. Wake up, have breakfast, get ready for school, try to make the bus, come home, have a snack, go out an play, come back at dinner when Dad got home, take a bath, watch TV, go to bed. And do the same the next day.

What conflicts do you remember?

Thats funny. Our lives were perpetual conflict.. Four boys fighting for survival with each other.

 

My Cousins

 

My Dad had one sister and my Mom was an only child. Dad's sister, Nell, had two children, a boy and a girl.

There names were James (Jimmy) Hammond and Marsha Hammond Fortunato Dorsey.

 

 

My Grand Parents

What reflections do you have about your grandparents?

I have pleasant memories of my grandparents, and I enjoyed them when we were together. But we saw them very occasionally--mostly holidays and vacations. I suppose this was partially because we lived in different states for part of the time. But there were times when a couple of them lived relatively nearby. Perhaps travel was not as easy at the time, or they had other lives. But I think was just unknowing. Not having come from large close family. They just didnt know any better.

In hindsight, I wish I had spent more time with them and known them more as an adult. It is one of the reasons I have chosen to move closer to my kids and their families. I want to have more of a relationship with my kids and grandkids than I had.

They Are People Too


Having been a parent and becoming a grandparent, it certainly gives me a different perspective on my grandparents lives. As a child, we see them as our parents parents. While they were a little younger when we were babies and young children, we come to see them as older people. They have their lives, but we are so caught up in our own that we cant see theirs.

Having recently organized a lot of their pictures, in addition to seeing them as 'grandparents', I can see them as younger people and the lives they led and the same daily ups and downs that we all have. They were kids, went to school, had boyfriends and girlfriends, got married, had kids, had jobs, had all the similar experiences we did or are having. It gives me a much greater appreciation for them. This influences the way I view my relationship with my grandchildren. I want to spend time with them as they grow up, and I want them to have good memories of the times they spend with me.

 

My Paternal Grandfather

Who was your paternal grandfather?

My paternal grandfathers name was George Bond. I didn't see him often growing up, because he had left the family when my father was about 7. But we did visit him in Texas and there was a time when he came back to live with my grandmother in Greer, SC, and another time in Jacksonville, FL. I saw him during those times. I dont know the story for his leaving or returning, but I remember him as a pleasant man. He was tall like my father.

As far as I know he was a farmer and worked in the orange orchards of Texas and Florida.

What impact did he have on your life?

Of course, he had an influence in my life because of the influence he had o my father, but I dont believe he had much direct influence apart from a few stories I remember about him.

What stories do you remember about him?

Orange Grove

One of my earliest memories of him was during a Texas visit. He worked in the orange groves and took us there one day. To me, the trees looked enormous. He held each of us up to pick our own orange and then took out his pocketknife (which I thought was cool, because no one I knew carried a knife) and cut a hole in the top of the orange for us to suck the juice out. I was so surprised at how good and sweet it was.

Solitaire

Another time, when I was around 9 or 10, they lived in Greer, SC in a mobile home. We were there on a Sunday one time and he taught me to play solitaire. I've never checked on this, but he told me that in Las Vegas, you could buy a deck of cards for $52 dollars (one dollar for each card). After you deal the cards, you could go through the cards only once (instead of every third card repeatedly), and for every ace and other cards you moved to the top, you would get 5 dollars. You also had to follow some other rules, like if a card could be played in more than one place, it had to be made on the first place from the left to the right. I used to play like that to see how much money I could win.

Go West Young Man

He used to talk about driving as going north, south, east or west. Hed say, You go east on such and such street. He was the first person I remember ever referring to directions that way and I didn't understand it at the time. He had a compass hanging from his rearview mirror. I assume he learned that in Texas.

Gidget

He had a chihuahua named Gidget that he loved dearly. She got out one day when they lived in the mobile home in Greer and was hit by a car. He was devastated.

His Death

When I was a teenager, I heard that he had died of a brain aneurism. I looked for my fathers reaction, but I never saw one. I have often wondered how he would have felt. He left when he was young and needed him and came back only after he had been successful. I can imagine there must have been very mixed feelings about that. Yet again, Dad was so kind-hearted that he may have forgiven him

.

My Paternal Grandmother

Who was your paternal grandmother?

My paternal grandmothers name was Margaret Baker Bond. Her maiden name was Margaret ??? Baker. She was a kind quiet lady, but she had a hard life and was a disciplinarian. She had to raise a daughter and son by herself at a time when women were not easily part of the workforce.

What impact did she have on your life?

What stories do you remember about her?

Seamstress for Pennies

Dad told me that when he was growing up, she worked in a sewing factory sewing collars and cuffs on shirts for 5 cents apiece. Assuming that was about 1945, that is roughly 88 cents in 2025. According to ChatGPT, groceries for a family were about $12.50 per week and rent was about $30 per month. I dont know how many collars and cuffs can be sewn in an hour with the machinery they had back then, but it seems like a tough way to earn a living with two kids and no father.

My father worshipped his mother, and his sister, for their whole lives. While I did not see his fathers death affect him, I definitely saw his mothers death affect him. He was very emotional..

Part-time Sitter

When I was somewhere between 7 and 10, she was living near us and would keep us during the day, while Mom helped Dad at the office. She had raised my father and his sister alone and she could be a tough lady. That must have been a very difficult job because we were always bickering and fighting with each other.

Muddy Bikes

One day, Scot and I were riding our bikes. The paving stopped at the end of the street, where the land ha d not yet been developed. The road continued but it was dirt. We knew the road wound all the way around some vacant land and then back into our neighborhood on the other end. It had been raining so the road was basically mud. We decided to ride through the mud.

At first the mud was not bad. We were making it through. But as we got to where we thought was about halfway around, the mud got deeper, and it started getting clogged between the tires and the fenders (bikes had fenders then) and the tires would not turn. We had to walk and drag our bikes in mud that was over the tops of our shoes. We tried to keep going, but we eventually turned around. We should have been home sooner and had no way to let her know where we were. So, she started walking through the neighborhood looking for us. It was not a small neighborhood. It took a very long time to get back to the paved part of the road. By coincidence she was waiting for us. Not very pleased with us, she had a switch (a thin green tree branch) and switched the back of our legs every so often all the way back to our house.

The Tattle Tail

As four boys within 4 years of each other, we argued and bickered ALL THE TIME. If we were out playing, and someone did something someone else didn't like, they would run in the house and tell her what the other was doing. Apparently, she got so tired of hearing it that she said the next person to come tell on someone was going to have to wear a tattle tail. I'm not sure if we understood what that meant, but Greg happened to be the unfortunate one to run in, tell on someone and find out. She cut a piece of old cloth and made him stick it in the back of his pants and wear it around as a 'tattle tail'. That cured us at least for a little while.

Coconut Cake

While she could be a disciplinarian, which we deserved, she was also a sweet lady. She remembered that I loved coconut cake and made me one for my birthday. 

She Loved to Fish

She also loved to fish. She and George would fish together. I remember her enjoying telling the stories of her fishing trips. She truly enjoyed it. And when she lived with Mom and Dad at the lake, she would fish, but they also visited a local pond that was easier and more frequent to catch fish. I have several pictures of her over the years fishing.

 

My Maternal Grandfather

 

Who was your maternal grandfather?

My maternal grandfather was Edgar (Ed) Wallace Davis. He was born ??? in ???

He was a handsome man. He was also proud but kind and gentle man, as I remember, but He could get feisty if pushed.. He was always frugal and conservative and organized. He dressed well and was always shaven (he always used an electric razor and was the only one I ever knew to use one). He seemed to always be finding humor in situations and laughing and enjoying being with people.

What impact did he have on your life?

What stories do you remember about him?

Cough Drops

When I was about 5 or 6 years old, we went to visit them in Atlanta . He was going to take me to downtown Atlanta with him to run some errands. I was very excited. I had had a cold or something and was coughing. He told me that when we were downtown, he would buy me some cough drops.

When we got there, the city was enormous to me. The buildings were tall, the streets were crowded, the traffic was noisy. I knew nothing about his errand and as we walked, I was solely focused on getting though cough drops. I dont know how many times I asked him, but apparently it was a lot and had gotten annoying, because as we walked down the street and I asked him when we were going to get the cough drops, he stopped, leaned down toward me and nicely but directly said something like, please stop asking me that, we will get them when I finish my errand. That was it. Wasn't too harsh, but I got the message. And, eventually, the cough dropsLudens.

Do You Have a Penny?


One night we were visiting and after dinner he was showing us his workshop. He was very proud of it and was very into explaining the details to us. His wife came in and said, "We are going to have some ice c ream, do you want any?" He didnt hear her and was very intent on continuing his explanation. She asked once and then again. When he finally realized she was talking to him, he whipped around in frustration with the interruption of his tour and said, "No I don't have a penny. What do you need a penny for?!" She calmly and sweetly repeated, "We are going to have ice cream, do you want any?" Realizing he had misunderstood, he quietly said, "No thank you" and sheepishly returned to his tour.

Santa Wont Stop

One year they came to stay with us for Christmas in our Creighton Street house. Scot and I shared a room, and Mom and Dad had put a cot in our already small room for him to sleep with us. On Christmas Eve, as bedtime came around, we were all shuffled off to bed and told to get to sleep so Santa could come. We went right to sleep, but at some point I woke up to his loud snoring. I must have inherited this from him, but he snored very loudly. I couldn't sleep. I tossed and turned and was so worried Santa wouldn't stop at our house.

Handyman

He was in the army for some time and also did some auto mechanic work. When he was older, he liked to do woodwork. He had a small workshop in his garage, and it was always kept very clean and organized. He would make wooden clocks and give them as gifts.

My Maternal Grandmother

 

Who was your maternal grandmother?

Martha Dora Stone Davis was Ed Daviss first wife. They divorced when Mom was about 7. (She later married and divorced a man named McDonald, changing her name to Dora McDonald.) She grew up in and lived in Atlanta. From the pictures I have seen of her when she was younger, she was pretty and full of life. But it seems later in life she became less happy. And you can tell from the pictures, and Mom's stories, that she was a good mother.

She worked for a long time in the Belk department store cosmetic section.

She had been seeing a man named Ford Little. I dont know the details, but he passed away, and she seemed to lead a lonely life after that. As a kid and teenager, I did not know enough to understand that when people are seemingly in a bad mood, it is likely driven by being quietly hurt inside. I learned this valuable lesson one time. I was married. We were visiting my parents, and my grandmother was there. She was very quiet and seemingly unhappy, but it was a state I had kind of gotten used to. I don't remember exactly what was going on, but Kerri turned to Mamma Davis and said something to her, inviting her to be involved with whatever we were doing, and I remember the pleasant shock of seeing her face light up with being included. It dawned on me what may have been going on all these years, and I regret not being more a part of making her feel included.

She lived in Greenville, SC near us, when I was growing up. When Mom and Dad would go out by themselves, we would go to her apartment, to spend the night. I thought the apartment was cool because it had a pool. She had a pullout couch that we would all pile onto and watch TV. She and I used to like to watch a show called Mannix. It was one of the first detective shows. We liked it because they always gave you enough clues to figure out who did whatever it was that had been done, and he always solved the case.

What impact did he have on your life?

What stories do you remember about her?

First Detective Show

When we were younger, she lived in an apartment. She would keep us when Mom and Dad went out. She and I used to like to watch the tv show called Mannix. It was one of the first detective shows where you had to analyze and try to guess who had committed the crime. We'd watch that together, all of us on the pullout couch. If we had not watched it together, we would talk about it when we got together. 

Different Inside

She seemed to always be quiet. From pictures I've seen of her, she was a very outgoing, confident person when she was younger. But it seems later in life she became less happy. She had been seeing a man named Ford Little. I dont know the details, but he passed away, and she seemed to lead a lonely life after that.

As a kid and teenager, I didnt know enough to understand that when people are seemingly in a bad mood, it is likely driven by being quietly hurt inside. She was very quiet and seemingly unhappy, but it was a state I had kind of gotten used to. It was only later that I realized that it was more than she was lonely and afraid. I regret not being more a part of making her feel included.

My grandmothers name was Dora Macdonald. She was also a very pretty woman when she was younger. She lived in Greenville, SC near us, when I was growing up. When Mom and Dad would go out by themselves, we would go to her apartment, to spend the night. I thought the apartment was cool because it had a pool. She had a pullout couch that we would all pile onto and watch TV. She and I used to like to watch a show called Mannix. It was one of the first detective shows. We liked it because they always gave you enough clues to figure out who did whatever it was that had been done, and he always solved the case.

Disrespect

This is not a story I am proud of, but I will tell it to provide some insight into my early life and hopefully growth in comparison. When I was a teenager in high school, my grandmother was at our house on Parkins Mill Rd. Mom had left and asked her to keep an eye on us. By then we didnt need babysitting, but she just wanted someone around. I think she had asked that I be home at a certain time, and my friend and I were in the driveway talking and getting ready to go. Mamma had been trying to tell me this several times as my friend and I were talking and moving around getting ready. At a certain point, when it seemed we were about to leave, she pointedly repeated what she had been trying to say, Youre Mom wants you to be home by (a certain time). I had never done this before, but I snapped back at her, I know. I heard you. I will! She was immediately taken aback. I was internally surprised, but I didnt show it. She immediately started crying and ran into the house. I dont remember, but I dont think I said anything then, but later I apologized. In retrospect knowing how sensitive she was, I feel even worse for having treated her that way.

 

 

My Maternal Step-Grandmother

 

 

Mildred Ivey Davis was Ed Daviss second wife. She was a sweet lady. She seemed to be always smiling and laughing. They lived in Atlanta, and while we saw them occasionally, it was holidays mainly. She was always laughing and smiling and joking. When we would go to her house and visit, she would always make breakfast for us.

Again, I learned most of what I know about her after I was grown, looking at pictures and asking questions.

What impact did she have on your life?

 

What stories do you remember about her?

 

Childhood

 

 

 

Preschool


What do you remember from this period?

First Home

Shortly after I was born, we moved from Richmond, Virginia to Spartanburg, South Carolina. I dont remember the Virginia house or the house we first moved to in Spartanburg. I think Scot was born in that first Spartanburg house. Then we moved again to the first place I remember, which was 612 Greenhill Road, also in Spartanburg. It was a very small house. I have only a few memories of this house. From pictures, it seems like it had two bedrooms, a kitchen, a den/living room and downstairs was a garage. In December of 2023, at Christmas, Mom and I toured Spartanburg and Greenville looking at all of the places we lived and talked about the things we remembered.

Early Daredevil

I was sitting at the top of the stairs on my tricycle, deciding whether to try it. I didnt know exactly what would happen, but the projection I had in mind was that it would be bumpy, but slow and controlled. I was hesitant, but eventually pushed my front wheel over the edge and it was anything but controlled. Everything was a blur until I found myself at the bottom with my mother running down the stairs to me. I have a scar on my forehead where I had to have four stitchesthe first of many.

Dad's Garden

Across the street and up from our house on a vacant lot, Dad had what I remember to be a big garden. I think I remember tall corn stalks and many other plants.. He bought me a plastic set of rake, shovel and hoe for me to help.

Dont Say Bad Words

I can still see Scot in the mirror from down the hall as Mom washed his mouth out with soap after he had called me a bad word. That was not something I wanted for me, so the threat of that always got me to straighten up.

Riding Horse

My first toy was a riding horse. I can vaguely remember riding it so hard that I could make it scoot across the floor.

I had a toy revolver that had plastic bullets that would really shoot. You could reattach the bullet part to the casing and reshoot them. That was cool.

Woodbine Terrace

In 1962 or 63, we moved from Greenhill Road. to Woodbine Terrace, another part of Spartanburg. This was another rental. From the pictures from back then, you can tell that it was a relatively new neighborhood, with few houses and several vacant lots. On my 2023 Christmas tour with my mother, we saw the neighborhood in its fully developed and much more mature state. But of course, my memories are of the early, raw state.

Good Morning Teacher

I only remember a little bit about kindergarten. It was off a main road, across the street from an elementary school. When we arrived in the morning, we would gather in the hallway outside the classrooms and sing to the teacher. It went something like, Good Morning, teacher, good morning teacher, good morning teacher, I can't remember the rest. In the afternoon, we would again gather in the hallway and sing, Good-bye teacher, good-bye teacher, good-bye teacher, we had a lot of fun!

Fall From the Car

One day on the way home from kindergarten, we were making a left turn. I was in the front seat, with no seat belt. Requiring seatbelts and securing children in car seats in the back seat would not begin until the 1980s. As we turned left, the front door opened and I started sliding across the big bench seat toward the door. Front seats used to be all one piece, not separate bucket seats like they are today. Mom reached out with one arm and caught me by the arm and managed to stop the car before disaster happened. But I can still see the pavement going by as I hang there looking at the ground/.

First Lesson About Fire

Dad cleared out the woods in our backyard and tried to control burn what was left, but the fire got out of control, and he had to call the fire department. After the fire was out, and without waiting long enough for it to cool down (not the sharpest tool in the shed), I started walking barefoot through the ashes. At first it was amazing because the ashes were soft and flowing as I walked. But I then stepped onto a smoldering coal and learned a tough lesson about fire. Mom put me in the bathtub and ran cold water over my foot.

The Bond Fire

What Dad had cleared, he piled into the vacant lot next door for a bonfire that night. The pile seemed gigantic to me at the time. I was less than five years old. All the neighbors came over and we roasted marshmallows and had a big get-together around the gigantic fire. The fire was so intense I could barely stand close enough with my outreached coat hanger to roast my marshmallow!

Learned to Ride a Bike

I learned to ride a bike at this house. Dad would hold the seat and run behind me until he thought I had my balance and let go. In the beginning, I fell right away. At least one time, he had started pushing, and I thought he was still pushing, but I couldnt hear him. I turned around and he was very far behind me. I fell. And we didnt wear helmets.when I knew he wasn't there. Of course, I eventually got it.

Spitting Black

There was a neighbor that lived down the street. Occasionally Dad would stop and talk with him. He would spit black and I could not understand how. I never asked and only realized many years later that he must have been chewing tobacco.

Blackberry Picking

I loved picking blackberries. Mom would make a blackberry cobbler with whatever I picked, and I learned to only pick the ripest ones, because the ones that are not completely ripe are bitter.

One afternoon while blackberry picking, I backed into a wasp nest and was stung three times. Dad rushed me home. Mom treated the stings and we went right back again. It did not deter me.

I later tried to get my kids into black berry picking, and they went once or twice, but they didnt seem to like it as much as I did!

.

Elementary School

 

What elementary school did you attend?

I went to Brushy Creek Elementary School in Taylors, SC. While the school has been expanded, the frnt in the pictures above are still the same.

How did you get to school?

I usually went to school on the bus that stopped right across the street from our house, but Mom would occasionally take me if I missed the bus.

I dont know if we were off schedule or the bus was off schedule, but I remember days standing in the cold seemingly forever and days when I was still putting my belt on as I ran out the door trying to get there before the bus left.

What do you remember from elementary school?

First Grade

My first-grade teacher was Ms. Hudson. I don't remember much except writing letters on those lined pages and getting stars for eating all my lunch!

Second Grade

Second grade was Ms. Burbage, and the only thing I remember is the reading set we had.

Third Grade

Third grade brought Mrs. Ford. She was big and mean. If someone did something against the rules, she would grab them by the upper arm and dig her fingers into the bone. One day she made my friend Dennis cry doing that. I don't think that would be allowed today.

People have come to believe this is a bad practice, but we would get stars for eating all of our lunch. I got a star pretty much every day, which might help explain why I wore husky size clothes until into high school. I always hated having to get the bigger sizes.

Fourth Grade

Mrs. Boehm was my fourth-grade teacher. I liked one of the girls in the class named Frieda. One day, Frieda was asking to go to the restroom. For some reason, Mrs. Boehm would not let her go. Eventually, as Ms. Boehm was talking, we all started hearing water splashing on the floor. Frieda could no longer hold it and had to let it go. I can only imagine her embarrassment and the trouble the teacher would be in today.

Fifth Grade

In my fifth-grade year, around 1970, our school was undergoing integration. Our class was informed in advance that three Black students would be joining us, and Mrs. Harrison, our teacher, arranged for one to sit beside me by placing an empty desk next to mine.
