It can be a little intimidating at first buying several thick text books that each are expensive and contemplating the amount of material that you will have to study and learn

Kappa Sigma

When I first went to USC, I went to a bunch of fraternity parties. The brother of a girl I had dated in high school was a Kappa Sigma. I met him at their party and he invited me back to the others. I went to the parties but knew nothing about joining a fraternity. At

the end of the party week. He called and invited me to join. I was very naive and knew nothing about what this meant. I was intimidated by it and told him I didn't want to join. He was very surprised. In hindsight, it is very unusual to turn down an invitation like that. I didn't give it much more thought until I started writing the answer to this question. Had I accepted and joined, I doubt I ever would have transferred to Clemson. I would have been too tied to the fraternity and school.

Panty Raids

One of my first experiences at Carolina was a panty raid. On a Friday or Saturday night, a group of guys would go by all the guy dorms and recruit participants. They would then go to the various girls dormitories and chant for the girls to throw down their panties. Like a naive prude, I didnt go, but I still remember they had a blind guy at the front of the crowd that was holding a pair of panties up in the air on his stick. He was having a blast and couldnt see a thing!

Muhammad Ali Fight

In September 1978, Muhamad Aali was scheduled to fight a rematch with Leon Spinks. We ordered pizza and a keg of beer at a friends house. Did not feel well the next day.

Poor Study Skills

When I first started classes, I had no particular study skills other than the poor ones I had in high school. I wouldnt even say I had any particular practices other than reading and maybe writing some random notes. In one of my first classes, Economics, I happened to sit behind this guy who had a ruler, a pen and a highlighter, and he was straight-line underlining and highlighting almost everything in the book. Everything was so neat. I didnt know what else to do and that seemed like what I should do, so I got the same tools and started doing the same thing. With practice I developed better study skills, but they were not great.

First Heartbreak

The first semi-serious girlfriend I had was in the summer before I went to college and my first semester. She was going into her senior year, and I had just graduated. I think I was partially holding on to something at home as I went off to college that I was unsure about, but I was head over heels about her. I wrote her letters from school. I came home almost every weekend. She came down to one football game.

One night I was at her house getting ready to go back to my house before going back to school the next morning. We were sitting on her porch, and she told me she wanted to break up. I was so infatuated I did not see it coming and it hit me like a truck. I was really devastated. Of course, whatever I tried to change her mind just made it worse.

Academic Turnaround

The first semester, probably because I was going home so much, I made a 2.8 GPA. After she broke up with me, I threw myself into school and working out. The next semester I made a 3.9 (should have been a 4.0 but this marketing professor from Europe that literally wore the same suit every single day to class and apparently didn't shower all semester wouldn't give me 1 point that I needed for an A.)

Best Shape of My Life

I also worked out and was in the best shape of my life. I had a routine. I would get up early for classes. As soon as class was over, I'd go to the dorm, drop off my books, change and go work out. There was a boxing speed bag that I learned to hit. I played pick up basketball with whoever was there. After the workout, Id shower, eat in the cafeteria then go to the library until about 11pm. Then I'd go home and go to bed and do the exact same thing the next day. My parents had the daughter of one of Dads business associates stay the summer with them. She would call me Le Hulk. We had a sprinkler system in the dorm with exposed pipes. I could do almost 20 chin ups with my legs at 90 degrees. I was fit.

Green Hole

Just outside Columbia, there was an old quarry filled with water called Green Hole, because the water is green.. The sides were sharp cliffs and they were very high. One of my roommates, was a diver in high school and he would do these crazy twisting dives. The rest of us would just jump. There was also a rope swing you could use to swing out over the cliffs and drop into the water. Thankfully, no one ever got hurt.

Late Night Exploring

I dont remember how I learned about this, but there were/are tunnels under the original business school buildings. They could be accessed through the manhole covers. A girl I was dating, and I went exploring one night going from building to building.

Transfer to Clemson

After 2 years, I was doing okay, but I was not thriving. I had a few friends, I did not date much. I was just sort of getting by. Dad must have noticed this, because he suggested that I might like Clemson more, and suggested that I try summer school at Clemson to see how I liked it. I enrolled, dormed in the tin cans (??) and took biology. I liked the campus. I like the class. And I met a girl in class that I liked and dated through the summer. So, I transferred to Clemson for the Fall semester.

The transfer turned out to be a great decision. I initially held on to favoritism for USC, but eventually changed my thinking on that.

Its actually quite scary how such important decisions can be made on such flimsy support. I think it suggests that almost no matter what the decision, things will work out.

The 1970s

I ended the 1970s with a transfer to Summer School at Clemson in May. By the end of the summer session, I was already convinced I would transfer to Clemson. I had enjoyed the class, met a girl and enjoyed the town. So I started the new decade at a new school that would come to make a tremendous change in the trajectory of my life.

Why did you transfer to Clemson?

I transferred because while I was doing okay, I was not engaging, enjoying and getting what I should have wanted out of college. I didnt see it as clearly as Dad must have for him to recommend I try summer school.

But after the first summer session, I was convinced to move. My loyalties to USC had not yet waned, that got me into trouble initially, but even that eventually came around and became fervent for Clemson.

Lost Credits

When I transferred to Clemson, I lost thirteen credits, almost a full semesters worth. Part of it was my D in math ☹, but I had taken high-level Spanish classes that were more than what I needed for the Administrative Management. major.

Kappa Sigma Round 2

While I had passed on joining a fraternity at USC, by the time my junior year rolled around, I had matured a bit more and wanted to take another look.

I went through rush and went to practically all of the fraternity parties on the quad. I had no particular preference going in. I knew nothing about any of them. But the guys I met at Kappa Sigma just seemed to be more down to earth. Good capable guys, but not arrogant. A couple of the fraternities I came to learn later had their own collective personalities, that would not have meshed with mine.

They offered and I joined.

Pledging

When you first join, it is called pledging.

So as a young pledge, as a way of getting you involved with the other members, they ask you to do errands for them.

They would have us park their cars. Parking was far away from the buildings, and it was always a trek to drive their car down there and then walk back. And sometimes we had to do several per night.

They would also have us go to the convenience store downtown, which was only a few blocks, but it was still an effort and sometime multiple times per night

Since I was joining as a junior, and already as old or older than many of the members, I didn't have to do as much as the others. They were intimidated asking me to do anything for them.

Arrogance Cost Me

In my junior year, the fraternity had what was called "Greek Day". All the fraternities would get together and compete in crazy games, that mostly involved drinking. After a full day of playing these games in the sun, everyone was tired and had had too much to drink. A guy in another fraternity started calling us names and cursing at us. We ended up getting in a fight. I had pretty much gotten the best of him and everything stopped. Everyone was standing around, and that guy blind-sided me, hitting me in the side of my left eye.

When I stood up, I saw two of everything. My friends took me to the doctor and it turned out that the blow had broken the bottom of my eye socket and the muscle that controls my eye was caught in the fracture keeping it from moving freely. They drove me to Greenville that night and I had an operation to fix it.

Because of the hit to the eye and the operation, I had a huge black eye. My fraternity brothers who loved giving me a hard time about it and one of them started calling me "Petey", after the dog on the show from the 1930's called Our Gang, or The Little Rascals. He had a big circle around his eye. Some of my fraternity brothers still call me that when I see them.

Being in a fraternity was great for me. It exposed me to friends and experiences that I would not have had otherwise. Many of those that dont join fraternities look down on them, and there are probably some good reasons for that. They can be exclusive and condescending to others. But, being in one gave more structure to my life outside of academics. The times I was not in a fraternity, I would occasionally get together with friends and go out, but often I didnt, choosing to not do much. In the fraternity, there is always a football game and after party to go to. Socials with sororities are arranged to facilitate mixing without having to go to bars. There are formal parties that give you the opportunity to dress up and experience that side of life.

Missed Being Grand Master

Another thing it prepared me for was taking on other roles. When we elected new officers, I volunteer to be the Grounds Chairman. The grass in front of the dorm was sparce and run down, so I rented a rototiller, got some guys to help and planted new grass, trimmed the bushes and picked up the trash. It wasnt much but it was more than others had done.

The next semester I was considered for Grand Master, which is basically the President of the fraternity chapter. I think I would have gotten it, but I didnt because I had gotten into an argument with another fraternity brother over the South Carolina game. For some reason, I still wanted USC to win the game, and they didnt. This guy was giving a hard time and I threatened to kick his ass. Not a very good thing to have done. And I think it is what cost me that position. I think it would have been very good for me.

The Phone Recovery

When I moved out of the apartment with Scot into Johnstone, I was paired with another random guy. He was in ROTC and majoring in engineering.

After I joined Kappa Sigma, I noticed one day that he had one of those old fashion phones where the dial was on the front and the receiver sat on top of it. It was painted in Kappa Sigma colors and had the insignia on it.

I didnt think much about it, but I mentioned it to one of the fraternity guys. His eyes lit up as he explained the phone had been stolen some time ago. They wanted to go after him, but I told them I had to live with him and to leave him alone. They did, but I took the phone and returned it.

My roommate came home later while I was studying, and without looking up, I said, Oh, I took the phone back. And without missing a beat said, Yeah, Okay. And that was the end of it. Haha! He knew!

What an unfortunate coincidence for him. He was an okay guy, but we were just different. We came and went for a semester and didnt really become friends. The next semester I moved into the fraternity dorm.

Where did you live during college?

The better question might be where did I not live in college. At South Carolina, I was in one dorm for the 2 years, but when I transferred to Clemson, I was in a different dorm every semester, sometimes 2 in the same semester.

I lived in 6 different places in three years.

    Summer school in the tin cans

    Fall in an apartment with Scot

    Spring in Johnstone

    Fall and Spring in Fraternity dorm

    Fall in married housing

    Spring in new dorm (We were the first to live there. When William went there over 20 years later, he stayed there with other baseball players.

I didnt realize how much I had moved around. But it didnt seem like that much. We had to take all our stuff home each semester anyway, so it seemed normal.

Nicknames

In my junior year, the fraternity had what was called "Greek Day". All the fraternities would get together and compete in crazy games, that mostly involved drinking. I'll tell another story about the records I set at Greek Day. But, after a full day of playing these games in the sun, everyone was tired and had had too much to drink. In the traffic jam leaving, a guy in another fraternity started calling us names and cursing at us. We ended up getting in a fight. I had pretty much gotten the best of him, and everything stopped. Everyone was standing around, and that guy blind-sided me, hitting me on the side of my left eye. I dropped to my knees. As I was standing up, I instantly knew something was wrong. When I stood up. I saw two of everything.

My friends took me to the doctor and it turned out that the blow had broken the bottom of my eye socket and the muscle that controls the eye was caught in the fracture keeping it from moving freely. They drove me to Greenville that night and I had an operation to fix it.

But, because of the hit to the eye and the operation, I had a huge black eye. One of my fraternity brother 'friends' started calling me "Petey", after the dog on the show from the 1930's called Our Gang, or The Little Rascals. He had a big circle around his eye. All I can say is it was my left eye, not my right eye like Petey's. Some of my fraternity brothers still call me that when I see them.

Keg Toss Champion

One of the Greek Games was the keg tossa competition to see how far someone could throw an empty keg. The first year I watched the games and noticed how the guys would try to shot put it. The most they could throw it was 10-12 feet. I knew I could do better. So, the next year I entered the keg toss competition.

As the other competitors continued with the same technique, they were still only throwing it a short distance. Everyone was gathered around very closely because there was no chance of getting hurt. When it came to my turn, I motioned for everyone to back up. They backed up a few feet. I motioned for them to backup more. They looked confused but backed up. And I kept backing them up until I was sure they were clear. They were all confused and questioning why I was backing them up so far.

Then I employed the modified hammer throw technique where I held the keg in both hands and spun around twice to build up momentum and then throw. The keg sailed over 25 feet! The crowd erupted and I won! The next year others were copying my technique.

Mistaken Bravado

Also, when signing my name, I always had a first initial, M. Alan Bond. When I was in college, in a fraternity, one of the things we as members did to get the new members to get to know everyone else in the fraternity, instead of just a few, was to have them visit each of the 'brothers' as they were called and have a conversation with them. Afterward, 'pledge' (new member) would ask for the members signature in a book to prove he had spoken to that member. One night after talking with a pledge, I signed his book. There was another member in the conversation, and when he started to sign, he noticed my signature and tauntingly accused me of signing "Mr." Alan Bond. I quickly explained and escaped further ridicule, but it is just one of the ways that being called by my middle name has been 'different'.

Orange Bowl

In the 1981-1982 season, Clemson went undefeated in the regular season and was set to play Nebraska in the Orange Bowl for the National Championship. Mr. Melton took us all in their van. 13 hours there and 13 hours back. It was thrilling.

We were cleaning the van and Kerri asked Sadie, Do you want to go to Miami? She said, Yes, and Kerri said, Well, you have to ask Papa? She walked to the front of the van and asked, Papa, can I go to Kerris ami? So cute.

What were your friends like in college?

My best friends in college were my fraternity brothers. Having spent 2 years not in a fraternity and three years in one, I highly recommend getting involved in one. It provides an instant social circle and many activities that would be close to impossible to create on your own.. Elizabeth and Caroline joined sororities and William had his baseball team, which was similar to a fraternity,. He likely would not have had time for a fraternity with his baseball practice schuel. We had a football player in our fraternity and we almost never saw him because he was with the football team. But the key is to have a structured social system.

Were the classes hard?

I never felt like the classes were hard. The only class I ever felt was hard and almost failed was math. First of all, it was my first semester in college and I made the mistake of scheduling it for 8am on Monday, Wednesday and Friday. I frequently missed class and did generally terribly on the exams. I am surprised I even passed.

Most of the other classes were more interesting and easier.

Who were your roommates?

At South Carolina, I was assigned a random group of guys from all overNew York, New Jersey, and of al places, Clemson. When I transferred to Clemson I had a random roommate for the summer, then roomed with Scot for a semester. Then had a random roommate in ROTC, then I lived another year with a friend of mine in the fraternity dorm. And then three of my fraternity brothers in a new apartment-like facility, that ironically, William lived in his first semester with baseball players 25 years later.

What traditions did you participate in?

Tailgating

Fraternity Parties

First Friday Parade

1982 was a very good year!

My Senior Year at Clemson was a very good year. The fraternity was the Fraternity Sing, came in first in the interfraternal council scoring (

Clemson won the national champsionship, we went on a great Senior Cruise

What was your schedule like?

I usually took 15 hours per semester, which was about 5 classes of 3 hours each per week. Monday, Wednesday and Friday classes were 1 hour each and Tuesday and Thursday classes were 1.5 hours each. So that works out to only about 3 hours each day of classes. I tried to schedule them for 9am or after and to be finished by 2pm. After that I would study if I had to or just do whatever else I wanted.

 

 

College Reflection

How did college shape you?

College is more than and education of knowledge. It is an incubator of experiences most removed from the real world that shapes your identity.

I am so thankful that I transferred to Clemson and joined a fraternity. The structure and experience was part of what I needed to mature and gain confidence. I think part of what Dad saw in me at South Carolina is that I was lost and had lost confidence. He was very astute to step in and help me make the change. It is one of the pivotal decisions of my life. It is ironic that after having planned to go to Clemson, changed to South Carolina, then back to Clemson. But, had I gone directly to Clemson, it would not have turned out the same.

What's one thing you wish you'd known before going to college?

Wow, there is a lot that I wish I had known before going to college. There is a lot I wish I had known about a lot of things. I was sheltered, but at the same time, was not taught, because my parents didnt know and I didnt know to get it from anyone else. Maybe this is the same for many, but, in general, I wish I had been more mature overall with a better idea of the overall plan. I was a sheep just going with the flow, reacting to every little thing.

What advice would you give to a family member about college?

College is a great experience for many reasons. It can prepare you for a career in many fields, although it is not going to prepare you for a specific job. This is something to understand. Many feel anxiety after college when interviewing because they still feel they do not know enough. That is correct. They do not know enough. But they are not expected to. They are expected to have an understanding of then field, but they are not expected to be able to perform a job independently without training. Employers are looking for intelligence, personal skills, social skills, and a solid grounding in the major field, but not expertise. That only comes with specific job experience.

         Get good grades

         Make good friends.

         Have a good time, but not too much of a good time.

         Dont go into debt. Getting out will take a long time.

 

What challenges did you overcome?

What life lessons did you learn?

What mistakes taught you most?

What successes encouraged you?

How did your goals evolve?

What social experiences shaped you?

Experiences that shaped me for better or worse were the fraternity that I did not join, the girlfriend that broke up with me, my drug experiences with my New York roommate and his New Jersey friend. The girl that I met right at the end of the Spring sophomore semester that I never saw again.

.

Graduate School

 

Did you go to graduate school?

Yes, when I graduated from Clemson, I went to graduate school at the University of South Carolina, in the Masters in International Business Studies (MIBS) program. It was a 2-year, intensive, year-round program that required learning a foreign language and completing an overseas internship where the language is spoken. I chose the Spanish track and interned in Colombia with E.R. Squibb and Sons, a subsidiary of Squibb Pharmaceuticals.

How did you pick a major in graduate school?

I studied international business in graduate school.

The Master in International Business Studies program was started at USC in 1977. That was the year I graduated from high school and the year Dads company won the Export Award in South Carolina. One of the founders came and met with Dad and explained the program to him. He shared it with me, thinking at the time that I would work for him after college in international business. That is not what happened, but that is how I first learned about and became interested in international business.

How did you choose a major?

When I was in about the ninth grade, one of the men that eventually started the Master in International Business Studies at the University of South Carolina visited my dad and explained the program and what they had planned. Dads company had been exporting products overseas and they were trying to generate support for the program. Dad told me about it and we had generally been considering that I might work for him when I graduated. I liked my Spanish classes, and it seemed like a good fit. But at that time the program had just started. Around 1974 or 75.

It wasnt like I was always planning to go to that program, because there were times that I did not think about it at all. When I first went to USC I was in the business program and I was taking Spanish, but I don't remember it being a frequent thought. Then when I transferred to Clemson, I must have completely forgotten about it. For one thing, I lost a full semesters worth of credits when I transferred because I had taken so much Spanish. I had to take other courses. I was also involved in a fraternity and college life in general. It was only when I was nearing graduation that I started thinking about it again.

Luckily, I was accepted. It was what launched me into international business, both working for the program after graduation, in my various jobs at Lanier and international travel in general.

Intensive Spanish

The program started with intensive language training in the first two summer sessions. It was definitely intense. I had had Spanish for three years in high school and had even taken a 500 level Spanish class in undergrad where the textbook was completely in Spanish, and it was still challenging. There were students that had never had any Spanish.

One day, relatively early in the classes, the teacher asked a student, How do you say, Good morning in Spanish. The guy was completely stumped, and he couldnt say anything for over a minute, but the professor would not relent. Finally, he said, Buenas Mananas, and the class erupted in laughter. Its Buenos Dias. Not the worst mistake, but pretty funny. He didnt make it.

Started Strong, Finished Middle

After the first semester of business classes, likely because I had been a business undergrad, I ranked 3rd in the class of about 80. However, after the second semester, I had dropped to the top third and I think I finished about in the middle. The few that were battling it out for number one were interesting. One girl/woman was cool and finished exams far ahead of anyone else, and there was a guy that wanted it so bad, and chain smoked every day. I think she finished 1st.

Internship

Cartagena, The Miami Beach of Colombia

I had a couple of classmates from Colombia and when they found out I was going to Colombia, they told me that Cartagena was the Miami Beach of Colombia. That description painted a modern picture of a beach and we wanted to go there for a few days vacation before we started the school position of the internship. I could not have been more nave.

The Arrival

On the trip down, I wore khakis, a white bottom down ana sports jacket and leather shoes. We have practically everything we owned in multiple giant suitcases. When we arrived, it was late afternoon and it was hot. The plane stopped on the tarmac and we had to walk down the portable stairs across the tarmac to the terminal. The terminal was open air, with no air conditioning. The view as we walked toward it was of hundreds of poor looking people holding on and peering through the bars at us. There seemed to be no organization. We managed to get our luggage and make our way to the taxis. All the taxis were dilapidated old cards. After stuffing our suitcases into he car. We head toward the city. On the way we passed waterways with handmade shacks built on the water and poor people sitting on the porches just hanging around. By the time we got to the hotel, it was getting close to dark. The room was small, open air, with no air conditioning. I had all the money I had in the world in a pouch strapped around my waste. I was petrified to go outside. We basically just got in the bed and tried to sleep. It was one of the worst nights of my life. The next day, thankfully, we were able to meet up with some of our friends who had also gone there. And that familiarity and their seeming lack of concern helped relax us. Lunch by the Pool.

One of the seemingly safe places to eat was at the restaurant by the pool. I was just getting used to pesos and the prices. I think tat the first meal, the total was 800 pesos and instead of tipping 80, I tipped 800. The next day we went to eat there again and it started to rain hard and the wind started blowing, The waiters picked up a table and held it in front of us to block the wind. It was only then that I realized I had over tipped by so much and that was the reason they were treating us like that.

 

Medellin

We spent the first 6 weeks in Medellin at a school called EAFIT taking more intensive Spanish. We lived with a family, and they took us to many places in and around Medellin.

On the weekends, we would travel with the project lead and his team to various places. Or we would meet up with friends from the program. We went to concerts, bullfights, festivals, farms in the mountains.

The Flight is Sickening

Medellin is in a valley surrounded my mountains. The flight in is sickening. The plane has to come in through an opening in the mountains, but still very high. The plane has to circle while losing altitude and it is bumpy and sickening.

Security

On our trips to the mountains, we were pulled over by the military, apparently they were looking for drug dealers.

Mountains on Bad Tires

The mother of the house allowed the 16-year-old boyfriend of her daughter take us on trips into the mountains. On one of our trips, we had to sop to get air in the tires. Later, as we were coming down the mountain in this little Renault 4, we was squealing the tires around the curves. I had to tell him to slow down or let us out.

The House

Small bedroom with Dick Van Dyke beds. Separate single beds. In the middled of the house was an area open to the sky. The floor was tile with a drain in the middle and when it rained, it would rain into the house. The whole house was open air. I came to enjoy the sound of the rain hitting the tile floor at night.

Im Not Eating That

Sometimes when I went to school, Kerri would stay home with the family and run errands. One day she told me they had bought something that she didnt know what it was, but said, Im not eating that.

One night, we were eating this soup, and I was just sucking it down. I hadnt noticed, but Kerri wasnt eating hers. We all finished and went to our room She asked me, How could you eat that? and I askded, What? She said that was the stuff I saw.

I had not noticed. It turned out to be tripe or cow stomach lining. Not appetizing to look at, but I couldnt tell in the soup.

 

Caf con Leche

A popular drink in Colombia is caf con leched (coffee with milklots of milk. Because Colombia is known for coffee, they made their own version of khaluha. On Fridays, we would get together with friends at their houses and drink caf liquor with milk. Their milk was whole milk. It came in a plastic bag. But it was very rich and delicious.

So Many Foreigners Here

In our classes, we were taught to be sensitive to cultures we were to be living in. Americans have a tendency to believe they are at home wherever they are. One day we got on the city bus from school to go home and one of the wives of another student said out loud in a complaining voice, There are so many foreigners here! She was serious and the rest of us just stared in disbelief. She was very self unaware.

Flight from Medellin to Cali

When we flew from Medellin to Cali, we flew with other students that were also interning in Cali or just going there to visit. We all went through a form of security they had back then and arrived in Cali. Before we split up, a couple of the guys were talking about smoking some pot before dinner. I asked them where they got it because we had not had time for them to buy any after we landed. They told me they had brought it with them. They had put it in a locked box in their carry on luggage. Luckily it was not discovered because they may have arrested all of us.

Hotel Dann Phone Call

When we first got to Cali, we stayed at the Hotel Dann. I made a collect call home. A collect call is where you call the operator, give them the number and tell them you want to make a collect call. They call the number and wait for someone to answer, If the call is answered, the operator says they have a collect call from you and they ask if they will accept the charges.

Of course, they accepted and we talked for about 20 minutes. When we checked out of the hotel, the hotel had charged us like $75 dollars for the phone call showing it as a direct call from our room. We argued, but they refused to change it.

Luckily we were staying in Cali and were able to get a copy of the bill from home showing the call had been collect. I went to the hotel and after some arguing got our money back.

Apartment Roommate

In Cali, there was another student with an internship there. We were friends. We decided to share a two-bedroom apartment. I dont have any specifics, but in hindsight, that was not a good idea. We were newly married, I was home during the day and away at night. He was having girlfriend problems at home. No specifics, but just not a good dynamic.

The Work

When I first arrived at my internship company, they had me start working with the inventory people. Im not sur exactly what happened, but I think they may have assumed that I knew more about inventory than I did.

Shortly, I was given a desk, in an open room full of desks and I just sat there for days. I would go in every morning and I would sit there and simply make notes to myself.

Thankfully, shortly after a week or so, there was a tem of people there modifying a software program for use in Colombia and other Latin American companies. The leader of the project was American. He invited me to see if I could help them with their work.

I bought a book (in Spanish) on programming in RPG II and taught myself some basic programming. I was able to help them write the code for screen displays and reports. This took some of the load off of the real programmers who were working on logic.

Late Nights

Because the programming work used valuable processing time. We worked from 4pm to 10pm every day. Wed go for dinner at around 7.

However, because I was learning, I had to test my coding. Several nights I would stay until 2 or 3am trying to get the code to work properly. I didnt feel it was dangerous. I would either walk or get a cab home. That did not make the wife very happy with me.

Earthquake

One afternoon I awas lying on the couch reading a book, holding it with one hand. As I read the pages started to shake up and down. We were having a minor earthquake. I cannot remember if it was around the same time as the Popayan earthquake, but it was minor.

Popayan Earthquake

Bullfights

Flower Festival

Pulmon

We went to a festival with my friend from work. There was a vendor selling meat. I assume I was making it known that I was concerned about the meat. IWhen I asked, what it was, he replied, pulmonlung! It wasnt lung, but he was giving me a hard time.

Silvia

Its Very Down

One of the drivers to our trip to Silvia was talking to Scot during one of our breaks on the long ride there. The mountains were steep and were very high. The driver didnt speak much English, but he pointed and said to Scot, Its very down.

Aguardiente

Aguardiente, is an anise-flavored (licorice) alcohol, like Greek ouzo, that the locals drink straight. They sit a little tables in front of restaurants and stores and at home, everywhere and drink from little shot glasses and talk. The name means fire water.

Pepper Steak

While we worked from 4-10pm we would eat dinner at about 7. We would frequently go to this nearby restaurant that had very good food. My favorite was steak aux poivre (pepper steak).

Waiter Cried

When we went to dinner, there were 5 of us and we were ordering god food. There would be several waiters waiting on us. Those that spoke native Spanish became friends with them. One night near the end of the internship, they explained to the staff that I would be leaving. I didnt see it at the time, but later they told me that one of the waiters had cried when he heard I was leaving. Apparently he was gay and had a crush n me the entire time. Thankfully I never knew that.

Basketball

Squibb had a company basketball team. One of the guys asked if I wanted t play with them. I said sure. I was taller than most and I helped the team a good bit.

We had a game against a rival company and they had a guy that was their star dribbler and shooter. I was to defend him and we had this little intra-game battle. He knew it and was getting his crowd on his side.

On one play, he got the ball and went on a fast break. I wasnt going to let him score so I chased him down and grabbed him around the waist rather than let him make a lay up. He fell on purpose, and acted like I had really hurt him.

I think we lost the game by a point or two.

Later I twisted my ankle badly and had to stop playing.

Money Exchange Fiasco

Nearing the end of the internship, we took a trip back to the city where we started, Cartagena. While we were there I heard that the black market exchange rate was a lot better than the official rate. It was against the law to exchange outside the official rate, but people did it. I was very nervous to do it, but people would approach Americans all the time, so there was no shortage of opportunity. I decided, I was going to do it, so the next guy that approached me, I said yes.

He asked how much I wanted to exchange and I think I said $100. That was a lot of money back then. I had no job and was living off money from my parents (another bad idea). He said okay and he asked me to hold out my hand. He put a relatively large bill down first, worth about $40 dollars. Then he started putting down a lot of smaller bills. Once he had put down the full amount, in a fast move, he looked over my shoulder and said, Policia, when I turned to look, he took the stack of money folded it, had me give him the $100. He said put it in your pocket, which I did, and we both separated.

We went to a restaurant to sit down. I pulled out the money to count it and found that in the sleight of hand he had removed the big bill from the bottom of the stack and left me with the small bills. I have never felt so sick and used in my life. In hindsight, it was a relatively small amount of money. But back then it was not. And on top of that the embarrassment of having been so deceived.

 

Career

 

What were your first odd jobs?

Probably led by my father, I seem to always be looking for ways to make money. Early, I several odd jobs. Later, as I got further into my career, I was always looking for an opportunity to be in business for myself. I dabbled in a few things, but

Bottle Collecting

When I was growing up in Taylors, included in the price of a bottled drink was a deposit of usually 5 cents. If you returned the bottle, they would give your deposit back. This was to encourage people not to litter and help save the bottling company money on bottles. But people still littered, throwing them out their windows on the way down the road.

I would ride my bike along the road and pick them up, take them to the store and collect the few cents per bottle. I would go in one direction on one side of the road one day. The next time I would go down the other side. I would usually get about 20 or so bottles. So that was about $1and I would use it to buy candy. From 1967 to 2024 inflation has averaged about 4%, making that about $10 in 2024.

Lawn Service

Later, I would drag my lawn mower around the neighborhood cutting lawns. I would ring the bell, ask if they wanted their grass cut. Theyd ask, How much? I would get somewhere between $3 and $5 per yard. In a weekend, I would make $10 to $15, about $140 in 2024! Not bad for an eight-year-old.

Cutting Grass

When I was about 10 or 11, I would get up on a Saturday morning and drag our push lawnmower with a can of gasoline around the neighborhood asking if I could cut their yards. The yards were always pretty big, and I would usually charge 3 to 5 dollars. Many people would say no, but several people would say yes. One weekend I made about $16 dollars. That was a lot to me then.
When we moved to Greenville, I occasionally cut grass there too. I had an older couple whose yard I would cut regularly. They were always glad to see me and always made sure I had water to drink. One day, I went to the front door of a house and asked them if they wanted me to cut their grass. They said, Sure, how much? From what I could see, I think I said, Five dollars. She asked, You sure? Dont you want to look at the side? I couldnt imagine the side would be very big, so I said, No, thats okay. She said, Okay. At that time, I had stopped dragging the lawn mower around. If someone said yes, I would go get the lawnmower. I said, Okay, good. Ill go get the mower and be right back. When I would cut a yard, I would usually start by cutting around all of the edges and then mow the middle. I started at the front and started cutting around the bushed and the edges, as I got to the side of the house, I looked up and the side yard was gigantic. It was bigger than all of a normal yard.

At that point, I turned off the mower and went home. I was too embarrassed to go and ask to change the price, so I just left and never returned. I felt bad about it for a long time. I still dont think it was the right thing to do. But as an adult I can put myself in their shoes and realize that they probably saw the part that I had cut and saw where it ended and laugh knowing that that was the moment I realized I had made a mistake.

Cutting Grass - I used to cur grass in the summer when I was 10 or 11 years old. I would take our lawn mower and a can of gas around the neighborhood asking if people wanted their grass cut. I am guessing now, the lots were maybe quarter acre lots, and I would cut most for 3 to 5 dollars. I remember one weekend I make like 15 dollars and I thought I had a lot of money.

Greeting Cards

I also used to sell greeting cards. In the back of the boy scout magazine there were ads for greeting card packages (mostly Christmas). Youd write in and theyd send you the sample. Id go around the neighborhood selling the cards, collecting the money and sending the orders in. Then the company would send the cards direct to the customer. I really didnt sell a lot. So, I stopped doing it.

What was your first real job?

My first real job was at Chick-fil-A in 1975, when I was 15 and a freshman in high school. I dont remember anything bout the interview. I only applied because my friend Ashley was applying. We had to wear khaki pants, a white shirt and I think we had to wear tiesnot sure about that. The Chik-fil-A was in McAlister Square. I think I was making about $4 an hour. My check for my first week was $14.50, but they took out 1.15 in taxes! Adjusting for inflation, $4 per hour is equivalent to about $25 an hour in 2025.

We werent supposed to eat while doing it, but when small pieces would fall off a bigger piece (or when we accidentally broke a piece off on purpose)), wed grab a pickle and sometimes a waffle fry, and pop that in our mouths. So good.

The best part of the job was preparing the food and working the cash register. The worst part of the job was the cleanup after closing.

I liked the job. I buttered the buns, battered the chicken, cooked the chicken and French fries, made chicken salad sandwiches, worked the cash register, mopped and cleaned tables, cleaned refrigerators--pretty much everything there was to do. We weren't supposed to, but when we were a little bit slow, we would break off a piece of chicken and put it with a French fry and a pickle and eat it--real daredevil stuff! Ha!

I suppose I learned some minor restaurant skills. I learned that you need to be careful who you trust with your job. I should probably have taken it more seriously.

First Real Firing

One day, I discovered golf team try outs were after school that day and I was supposed to work. I called the boss and asked if I could have the day off. He said only if I could find someone to work for me. I said great and called my friend who also worked there, explained the situation, he said yeah, no problem.

So, I went to the tryout. Didnt make the team. Came home, took a shower, and ran by to pick up my friend. We went out like we normally did, and went home.

The next day we were both supposed to work, so I picked him up and we went in together. When we got there, one of the people said, Hey, Mr. Ball wants to see you guys upstairs. Upstairs was up a ladder that led to an office in the ceiling of the back of the restaurant. So, we both climbed the ladder. As soon as we got up there standing in front of his desk, dressed in our mandatory khaki pants and white shirts, Mr. Ball said something like, We don't need boys like you here. Youre fired. I said, What? Why?

He said, You were supposed to work yesterday, and you didnt come in. I said, Yes, I know, I called, and you said I could get someone to work for me. and I looked over for Ashley to step in. He was staring at his shoes. He had not gone in. And, we had gone out the night before and he had said nothing about it. (I cant remember if I asked him how it went and he lied or I just never asked.) So, I stopped my explanation mid-sentence. Ball said, Yeah, we cant have that. or something like that.

So, we climbed down the ladder and started heading out. Ashley was hemming and hawing like he always did if he ever did something wrong. He was a master BS artist. But, by the time we got to the parking lot, I was over it. He knew of a party that was going on that night, and we got ready and went to it. The ease of being a teenager.

In hindsight, it seems odd he would fire us both for a scheduling error like thatone person not coming to work. Perhaps he was not happy with us for other reasons. I was a15-year-old kid, whod didnt need the job, had never had a job and I took my work seriously, but I dont think Ashley did, and that could have been assigned to me.

Screen Printing

After a trip out West, Dad became interested in the screen-printing business. He started one and I would work there helping out with odd jobs. Cleaning--the screens and sometimes printing the shirts. This came in handy later in college when I needed to make some extra money.

Pool Cleaning

After Dad sold his business, he was looking for other businesses to be in. He started a recruiting agency called New Careers and bought a house for the office that had a pool in the backyard. The pool had not been cleaned in a very long time. The water was so black you could not see below the surface.

Dad gave me a job cleaning the pool. I spent several days scooping, cleaning filters and applying chemicals until I had the water back to pristine condition.

When the job was complete, he let me have a pool party with a bunch of my friends. The neighborhood was fairly quiet, and the neighbors called the police, but they just asked us to turn down the music. Great fun!

 

 

University of South Carolina

 

Associate Director of Corporate Relations

My first job out of graduate school was with the MIBS program I had just graduated from.

Like a lot of things early in life, I didnt think too far ahead. While I was in graduate school, there had been this not often discussed assumption that I would work with Dad in some capacity, but nothing specific had been discussed. So, I suppose I was unconsciously relying on that, but should have been giving it more thought.

However, during my internship, I got a call from Mom and Dad, and they let me know that he had sold the business! This was great news, but I had not given the first thought to working for anyone else, and I would only have a couple of months to work on this during class when I got back to Columbia. So, I pretty much did nothing.

I signed up for a couple of interviews on campus, and I had an interview with Citibank in New York. But I was unprepared. The Citibank interview was interesting. Later because it helped me understand interviewing and myself better. We had a fine interview.

I think I answered his questions well. I was dressed well and was very buttoned up for the time. But, at the end of the interview he told me he enjoyed the interview, and thought I was an excellent candidate, but he didnt feel like he knew me well enough personally.

I didnt know how to respond at the time to try and open up for him more, which I later would have learned to do, It is another example of my early naivete about the way the world worked. The conversation ended, and I left.

So, after I graduated, I moved back home to figure out what I was going to do. Within the next few weeks, I got a call from the current Director of Corporate Relations, also a former student, offering me the job of Associate Director of Corporate Relations. I would assist him in his work, as the program was growing. With no other options and the offer of $25,000 per year salary ~$xx,000 in 2025), I was anxious to take it.

Speaker Program

Part of the job was to bring speakers in from international companies to speak with students about international issues. It was not that easy to get speakers because we expected the companies to pay for their own travel. Some would do it if they could charge it to their companies. But others wouldnt do it. But we had a list of executives that had come before that would come again and I was able to find new ones to fill in.

Alumni Relations

I was also responsible for Alumni Relationsmaintaining the database updated, which is harder than it sounds. And communicating with them via newsletter to try and keep them involveddonate money or come back and speak.

The job in total was challenging for me as a recent graduate of the program, because I was not experienced, was not working in the glamorous jobs they all aspired to and was the same age as many of them.

I redesigned the program logo, published a MIBS newsletter, learned how to send Telexes.

Ancient Communication Method

Almost no one remembers sending telexes, but before fax machines, the only way to send electronic messages internationally was by Telex. It was a typewriter connected to a

paper tape, and each letter would be punched as a set of holes on the tape. If you made a mistake youd either have to backspace through the last few characters if there were not many, but in most cases you had to start completely over again because the deleted letters would appear as blacked out characters on the received message.. Once you had this long tape with illegible holes in it, you would dial the international number to the recipients Telex machine and feed the tape into the reader. Once connected, the reader would pull the tape through sending the message to the other machine, where it would print out. It could take minutes to hours for the message to actually send. Often it would fail, and youd have to send it again.

This was well before word processors. I arranged all of my international meetings by typed letter and telex machine. Very time consuming and tedious.

Really?

Speaking of not having word processors. When I was still in school trying to get a job before graduation, I had to type several one page letters. Kerri helped me type them. I made a mistake signing my name to one of the letters, and because there was no such thing as a word processor, where the text could be saved, I asked her to type the letter again. It was only one page and the typewriter we had did have an automatic correction tape (before that, correcting mistakes on a typed page was a ridiculously manual process in hindsight), it would not correct it cleanly. She was not happy with me asking her to retype the letter.

Director of Latin American Operations

After the first year, the professor who had been handling the Spanish track students and placing them in internships in Latin America decided he didnt want to continue, so they added Director of Latin American operations to my title.

My job was to place American students in internships with companies in Latin America. We had a set of companies that took students every year, but economic issues would come up and a company would not renew, and the program was growing so we had to find new companies. I spent several weeks sending letters and Telexes to companies setting up a six-week trip to Argentina, Chile, Colombia, Venezuela and Mexico to meet with companies and basically sell them an intern. I was happy I was able to place all the students in internships that paid a stipend.

After the second year, I can't remember exactly how it worked out this way, but I also had to take on the placement of the last few interns in the US. Foreign students coming here to study and intern. I think the director was either sick or leaving the university. As the deadline approached, we had a couple of students without company intern commitments. Ultimately, we had to offer one student for free. Typically, they would pay what amounted to a very small stipend and usually the students more than paid for themselves. But it all worked out.

The student we had to place for free was a Korean student that was very intelligent, but

had a difficult time with English. They were reluctant to take him, but once he was there, they loved him because he was very smart. After his internship, he gave me a Korean fan as thank you for helping him, which I still have in my collection of souvenirs.

Dale Carnegie

I was not particularly good at public speaking. I was very nervous every time. I had things written out, but I could not even read the notes smoothly. It actually became a game of the students to listen until I made a mistake. In one of my last introductions, I almost got through the whole thing without a mistake and the pressure became to much. I made a mistake and they all basically cheered. Thankfully one of the professors offered to send me to a Dale Carnegie class. It was a class about speaking in front of people, but also other personal development areas. I took the class once, then was offered to come back and work as a graduate assistant for 2 other 6 week-classes, Helping the leader and other students taking the class. That was one of the most important personal development classes I ever took. It gave me the skills to speak with others in many settingsone-on-one, meetings, presentations, financial reorts, etc.

Government Waste

Thankfully, it appears that Trump is willing to start taking on the incredible waste, fraud and abuse in government, but it has been a well-known phenomenon for decades.

I had my first experience with it at USC, At the end f the year, the secretaries came around with office supply catalogs and informed us that we had not used all of our office supplies budget for the year, wo we should go through catalogs and order whatever we thought we might need. Everyone in the business department, which was every professor and administrator in the 5-stoy building, ordered anything they could think of. Desks, credenzas, bookshelves, chairs, day plannerseverything. When they totaled everything that had been ordered it was not enough to spend the amount budgeted, so the TRIPLED it! When the stuff came in there was not enough room to put it all, so it was stuffed into closets and any space they could find. Having been exposed to my fathers business and the need to save money and be efficient, this disgusted me. It always has. I am sure that this is just a small example of the massive waste, fraud and abuse that has gone on probably since the beginning of government.

NCR

Financial Analyst

After about three years with the MIBS program, I started thinking I needed to either get into business or this might end up my career. NCR hired me as a Financial Analyst in Columbia. The location was making the best-selling multi-user UNIX machines in the world at the time. The Controller was highly regarded for running a tight ship. We did analysis of financials and manufacturing variances, plus pricing proposals for big deals. While I was now working in a corporation, I remember having lunch with a co-worker and talking about starting our own businesses. One of the most distressing things about that job was that they had promoted a financial analyst to a management position, and he was not at all prepared for the job. You could see the fear in him whenever he had conversations with us.

Thankfully, while it was a good learning experience, I was only in that job for about.9 months. It was a good stepping stone to get out of the university, but it was not international and the only promotional opportunities would have been internal promotion to a manager job of other analysts or a transfer to Dayton, Ohio, which was not at all appealing.

Shortly after I left, AT&T bought NCR and I do not know what happened to the business or the people.

 

Harris/3M

 

International Financial Analyst

On Christmas Eve, about 2 weeks after Elizabeth was born, a guy I had met during my internship in Colombia called about a job with a joint venture between 3M Company and Harris Corporation called Harris/3M.

While Xerox introduced the first plan paper copier, 3M was the first company to introduce thermal paper copiers beginning in the 1950s and had built a worldwide network of subsidiaries to distribute their products. Harris had purchased the Lanier brand of dictation equipment and partnered with 3M to utilize the subsidiary network to expand its distribution.

The position was Financial Analyst for all international companies outside the US and Europe - called ROW (Rest of World). including Canada, Latin America, Asia-Pacific and Middle East. It seemed perfect - financial work plus Latin America where my language skills fit.

The reception at home was instantly negative. The move would take us further from Rock Hill, where her family was. We were already an hour away and this would make it 5 or more. I was devastated - since 10th grade I'd been learning Spanish and getting an international degree, and this was the perfect opportunity. I couldn't take it because of the distance from her family. I called the guy back and declined.

I was in shock and despair for two days until she reluctantly agreed. I had to call and convince the guy to reconsider me since I had already turned it down. Thankfully he did and I got the job.

As the International Financial Analyst, I officially reported to the Corporate Controller, but exclusively supported ROW. I never asked, but I can only assume that it was a budget or political (or both) issue. But all of my work was with ROW. It was a great job. I travelled to each of the subsidiaries with the rest of the senior management team and I got to learn all about these reviews. 3M was known for their management programs and had a prescribed method for performing these reviews. I also go to see how management interacted with the local management and vice versa. I also got to learn about the corporate and division interaction.

Run In

ROW had a Financial Manager, but Over time, as I learned the business, the president of the ROW division would come straight to me for information. I mostly funneled these requests back through the Financial Manager.

However, there was a time that I replied directly to the President and did not involve the Financial Manager. He was smart and capable, but he was also a hot head. He came to my office after he discovered he had been left out and told me in no uncertain terms that he was the Financial Manager and that all financial information was to flow through him. While I was angry, I was also a bit afraid. I was literally shaking from both feelings. I had never had that happen. I was technically in the wrong and it was a very unsettling experience that I learned from the hard way.

Finance Manager, ROW

I dont know exactly what preceded it, but it was likely a combination of poor internal controls and his hot-headed nature. The Finance Manager that had hired me into the company was fired, and I was promoted to Finance Manager for ROW. One of the issues that had plagued the division was financial mistakes and failed audits. The previous manager was a good business guy, (he amazed me at how he could look at a page of numbers and instantly see if there were any mistakes or anomalies) but not much on organization.

We had an internal audit group that would tour the subsidiaries and test whether their controls were adequate. Often, they were not. So, the first thing I did was take the 3M and Harris policy and procedures manuals and integrate them and translate them for use in all the ROW subsidiaries. It was a huge project. I toured each of the subsidiaries and ensured that each Finance Manager had read and understood the policies and had the procedures in place to adhere to them. Quickly, the financial mistakes and errors ceased and the audits improved.

European Controller

While Row had had its financial issues, the European division was larger and had similar problems, so their mistakes were even more significant to the company. Based on my success in ROW, they asked if I wanted to become the Controller for Europe. Based on the reaction I had received about taking the ROW job and moving, I was very concerned about the reaction I would receive at home. Thankfully, the reaction was more accommodating, and I accepted the job. We moved to Brussels, Belgium in January 1990.

Move to Europe

In 1990, we moved to Belgium. The position meant taking on the same responsibilities I'd handled for Latin America, but now for the European division. It was a major step up in terms of both responsibility and cultural adjustment.

My role as European Finance Manager involved extensive travel throughout the continent. I was learning not just about different business practices, but also how to navigate multiple cultures and ways of doing business. Each country had its own approach to both business and social interactions.

The Gulf War

While in Europe, the Gulf War started. One of the few channels we could get was CNN and we watched it constantly. There was little effect on us, but there was a heightened sense of security. One morning we have a bomb threat called into out building and we all had to evacuate. With nowhere else to go and not knowing how long the threat would last, we all walked a few blocks down the road to an Italian restaurant and spent the afternoon eating and drinking delicious food.

Ask For What Youre Worth

When we first arrived in Belgium, the company gave me a housing allowance and asked us to go find something. After 2 days of looking at everything in this price range, we cold not find anything that was remotely acceptable. The company tried to convince me that this was adequate and I should keep trying. I didnt directly threaten it, but I suggested that we would not move there for that. I didnt know how much we needed, but it was a lot more than what they were offering. They increased the amount and we were finally able to find something that was adequate.

Later we met other Americans working for European companies, particularly the 3M parent company. They were living in huge homes, and while I also was provided a nice car their salary cost of living adjustments were far greater than mine. They were living like kings and we were having to reduce our standard of living. Had I known what I know now, I would have been even more demanding.

A lesson I wish I had learned sooner is that companies have the resources to spend and pay, but they will scrimp and save and force the savings on employees if they allow it. The money I saved them and earned for them over my time there was worth far more than what I was paid.

For the promotion, the company gave me a raise over what I was making, but that was from a low starting point. I started as an analyst, had minor annual raises for 2 years, then a promotional raise to Finance Manager for a year, then this promotional raise. I was the controller in Europe for a salary of $53,000 in 1990, which in 2024 is about $128,000. There was a cost-of-living differential applied to that, but this is a ridiculously low salary for the position.

Finance Director, International

Part of the reason I returned from Europe was because the company was going to consolidate all of the subsidiaries outside the US into one international division and close the European office and they didnt want me to get caught in the reductions. So, I returned to the US working on special projects with the Controller again until the consolidation was complete. When it was, I was named Finance Director, International, reporting to the new division CFO.

Not Ready for CFO

After I had returned from Europe, I had a conversation with my father about my work. He asked me if I felt like I was ready to be CFO of the division I was working in. At the time, even though I had been back and forth to Europe, I did not feel like I was ready. I still saw the job bigger than I was. Or I saw myself smaller than the job. It was only a year or so later that I left Lanier for a CFO job. It was rough and I was not ready, but I eventually grew into the role and ultimately felt extremely comfortable in a leadership role.

Vice President, Business Development & Strategic Planning

Around 1995, I had been with Lanier for almost 7 years, the stock market was exploding with companies going public and while I might not have felt ready for the CFO job at Lanier, I was anxious for something with greater opportunity. I interviewed for and received an offer to become the Controller for an international trading company based in Atlanta. They traded food and other commodities all over the world, did business in multiple currencies and were looking to grow bigger. I accepted the job but asked for a month to close things out with Lanier. While I accepted, there was something that worried me about the job and company. I had the sense that they had not been completely open about the state of the accounting. I was also worried there were some problematic transactions. Back then, the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act was in place prohibiting bribes to officials, and a problematic aspect of international business was getting officials to do their jobs when you needed them to. And this often required bribes. Given the number and types of countries involved, I just had a sense there might be problems. A day or so before my last day at Lanier, the division head asked me if there was anything they could do to keep me. I said something like, I dont know, what do you have in mind? He said, Okay, good. Let me get back to you. He came back and offered me a promotion to vice president for business development and strategic planning. That was basically the dealer acquisitions we were doing all over the world to add to the presence we had in each country. Given the issues I feared at the trading company, I accepted. I called the recruiter that had placed me and told her I was rescinding my acceptance. She was not happy. She threatened to sue me for her fee. I checked with Laniers legal counsel and found there was nothing she could do.

While in this job, in addition to laying out the divisions first formalized strategic plan, I worked on an interesting acquisition in France. Acquisitions in France are complicated because labor laws protect workers from being fired for many reasons, and severance payments are higher than those in the US. Also, valuing the customer base had several variables including contract terms, estimates of renewals and price changes (increases), etc.

As it turned out, the promotion was only a temporary solution. Which reminded me of what the Lainer Chairman would always say. Once someone has gone so far as to submit a resignation, they are already gone, no matter what you do. I didnt fully agree with him until I had experienced this myself. Once I had made the original decision to leave, while they made it interesting to stay a little longer. It was inevitable that I was going to leave. I found this to be true with other employees over the years. Lanier needed to hold on to me for a little while longer, which they did, and benefitted, but I ultimately decided to leave.

 

SQL Financials

 

Vice President, Finance & Administration, CFO

SQL Financials was a startup ERP software company in the new and exploding software and technology market in the mid 90s. SQL was in the name because at the time, it was the most cutting-edge software/database tools available. Startups were getting up and running everyday and everyone was trying to go public. Until this time, companies were primarily valued in the market based on economic factors of revenue, profits and cash flow. But a mania had taken over the market and this was a period when companies with zero revenue, negative profits and negative cash flow were being valued at ridiculous and increasing levels. It seemed that all one had to do was promote an idea and get a tremendous valuation, even before proving the idea with revenue.

I did not know enough at the time, but despite its hype, the company had an architecture and scale problem with the product, and they were competing with many other small, medium and large ERP systems that were far more capable. They were burning through cash, and the investors were hesitant to put in more, so they implemented strict cash controls.

Despite the product and cash issues, the company built and leased a new building, one of my projects.

Its a longer story, but amongst all the other problems, I overlooked one of the conditions of the loan and cash dropped below the arbitrary one million dollar threshold. They used this as a reason to let me go, then hired another CFO to wind down the company. The SQL Financials shell company remained on the shelf for several more years, and because of its significant losses that could be used for tax purposes, was used to merger with another online purchasing site, like Amazon (there were many wanna-be Amazon startups, called Clarus. The shares that I had received for pennies I eventually sold for over $600k.


Caribiner

 

Vice President, Finance, CFO

When I left SQL Financials, I had to find another job. I came across a small ad in the newspaper by a company called Caribiner International looking for a divisional CFO in Atlanta. Having worked for corporations with buildings and modern offices, the Caribiner Atlanta office was a 2-office rental that was overstuffed with old furniture and boxes of files. The side door entrance would stick and had to be yanked open. It was quite a contrast.

But the Division President was an interesting, polished British guy, and the business was interesting. It was essentially a business creative services business. It employed talented people to do anything from create graphics to plan and hold small sales meetings to large shareholder meetings, to product launches, where they leased a coliseum in Detroit and built out a $10 million dealer experience when they launch the new Thunderbird car. The industry had been populated by many standalone creative production companies in major cities around the country. When large corporations wanted to execute projects in different cities, they had to employ different companies, with different contracts, procedures, personnel, payment arrangements, etc. The founders idea was to wrap these studios up into a pan-national organization where large corporations could deal with one provider. The company had offices in New York, Atlanta, Boston, Chicago, Dallas, San Francisco. Their clients were Coca-Cola, Honda, Ford, Pepsi, etc.

Each office had a Finance Manager, and my job was to work with each of them to ensure all accounting was done properly, analyze results and guide improvements throughout the division. It was a very challenging and enjoyable job. I had never been around such creative people and such a unique type of business.

I joined the company with the understanding they were going to move the headquarters from New York to Atlanta, and the insinuation that I would be in line for the CFO position for the whole company when that took place. The existing CFO wasnt going to leave New York and would have simply retired. They moved into a nice office, hired a CEO-to-be in Atlanta and began putting elements in place. However, the company made a large, ill-advised acquisition and the new CEO and the existing CEO had a disagreement. The new CEO was fired, and my boss was transferred to New York. They wanted me to stay on and asked that I commute to NYC three weeks a month. I did that for about a year and a half. They then asked me to move to NYC. I considered the idea, taking a house-hunting trip over a weekend, but the idea of living so far outside the city, having to commute over an hour per day, the impact on the kids in that environment, plus the fact the company was in a class action lawsuit over the bad acquisition and some projections the CEO had made to Wall Street, I decided to decline. I agreed to work from home through completion of their budget and take a six-month severance to find something else.

Ill-Advised Acquisition

I mentioned the ill-advised acquisition and thought I would explain it. The services business that Caribiner ran was not very resource intensive. It had a lot of people and some supplies, but did not have much invested in physical assets, like inventories. One of the services that they would frequently outsource was the audio/visual equipment that was needed to put on the various productions they put on. Of course, the provider was making a profit on the rental of the equipment to Caribiner. The idea was to acquire the provider, own the resources and book the savings as profit. The idea had several flaws. The business was extremely capital intensive. Audio/visual equipment had to be purchased all over the country and stored in facilities. The equipment is not stationary, constantly being moved from location to location, and is subject to damage and loss. The obsolescence and shrinkage factor was high. A nuanced difference is that the audio/visual business was privately-owned and the owners had a vested interest and years of experience in making sure their equipment was secured. Under a corporate umbrella, managers tend to be less invested and therefore attentive to the security of the assets. Therefore, the shrinkage from damage, loss, or theft increased dramatically. Stuff was just disappearing. Companies have varying competencies, and managing far-flung assets was not a competency of Caribiner. The CEO then communicated some financial projections that turned out to be optimistic, the stock tanked, and the class-action lawyers swooped in. It was a shame, because the business had great potential.

 

McKesson

 

Vice President, Finance

When I left Caribiner, I immediately started looking for another job. I remember sitting at my desk in the Edgewater house doing the search, relatively confident, but still not knowing what was going to happen, while the family continued about their business. I initially had a conversation with McKesson about a job, and the talks seemed like they were going to immediately work out, but the talks mysteriously slowed, they said there was a delay and then the conversation completely stopped without a word. A few days later, news came our that the company had found accounting irregularities, had to restate revenues. The stock crashed from $67 to $18a huge loss in valuation for one of the largest companies in the world. The CEO President, CFO, Chief Legal counsel and others had been fired. That explained the silence.

I continued to search but with no concrete success. At one point, I interviewed with Equifax. I believe they were prepared to make me an offer, but I got the sense that the title sounded good, but the people seemed a little too inexperienced and I got the sense that the job itself would be a lot of lower-level intricate analysis. So, I told them I was not interested, believing I could find something better.

The six-month severance was coming to an end when I saw a new ad for a position with McKesson. The new management was rebuilding the finance team. I was originally hired as Director, reporting to the Controller, responsible for the largest portion of the software products, but was promoted within a few months to Vice President, Finance, reporting to the new CFO, to lead the entire Financial Planning & Analysis (FP&A) team. Over the next seven years, I implemented systems and processes, hired people, completed acquisitions, and built a finance team that frequently won awards and received glowing comments for their capabilities and service to the Company.

During the 90s, there were many cases of companies falsifying revenues and expenses, such as McKesson, WorldCom, and Enron, among others. The Sarbanes-Oxley Act was enacted, putting requirements in place for companies to implement strict controls over their accounting processes to help stop these disasters. One of the themes of my work was putting these controls in place. It was a massive and challenging undertaking, but one in which we excelled. Our audits from the very beginning were stellar.

Chief Financial Officer

When I started as VP of Finance, they shortly hired a divisional CFO. He and I got a long very well. From the beginning, he claimed that he was going to work three years and retire, allowing me to succeed him. Long story short, three years turned into 7 years.. And it wasnt his stepping down. It was more corporate politics. It was the way he was forced to leave and my promotion that soured our relationship.

The position was challenging from the beginning. With the departure of the CFO, they also replaced the President of the division with a guy from one of the subsidiaries that I had had some business disagreements with. Its a long story, but he would not commit in his budget to revenue that was practically guaranteed. When a customer cancels a contract, it always takes a long time for the hospital to relace the equipment. N the meantime, they are required to continue paying. The business had over $12M in annual revenue contracts that while officially cancelled, would like have run for a least three years, but he refused to include even $1 in the plan. As a result, the guaranteed revenue offset the other shortfalls of his plan. The corporate politics aspect of it, is that I explained this to the then President, and she allowed him to under commit the budget, because it helped her undercut her budget to corporate!

Jeff Campbell, the CFO, once said in front of me, not realizing I was over 50, "We need to get rid of all these 50-year-olds and replace them with younger people." I probably should have sued for age discrimination, which it clearly was, but I didn't.

 

Given Imaging

 

Chief Financial Officer, Americas

In late 2013, I received a call from a human resources guy I had worked with at McKesson. He was then head of Human Resources for a company called Given Imaging, Its primary product was a miniature camera in a pill-shaped casing called PillCam. It was a potential replacement to invasive procedures like endoscopy and colonoscopy. The patient would swallow the pill and the device would transmit video wirelessly to a recorder worn on the belt. The job was CFO of the Americas division, responsible for all of North and South America.

Friends Can Create Blind Spots

The president that I reported to had hired a friend he had worked with at their previous company and put him in charge of Latin America. From my experience with Latin America, I knew something about the culture and how things were done. I would talk with him and the president about what was going on. It became clear to me that the guy really did not know what he was doing. In as an objective way as possible, I explained this to the president, but his friendship and loyalty to the guy kept him from seeing it for quite a while. However, he did eventually see it and he admitted that he had not been able to see it because of their friendship.

Dune Medical

 

Chief Financial Officer

In late 2017, I was referred by my boss at Given Imaging to an opportunity with Dune Medical, a portfolio company of the board of his then company.
