The product was a medical device which had the potential to help physicians identify clean margins during breast cancer surgery, helping them ensure they removed all of the cancerous tumors and avoid follow-up surgeries. Despite the potential, the companys previous 10 years had been filled with business mistakes. The two biggest were the failure to update the technology over the period and the failure to complete a clinical trial required by the FDA, proving the devices efficacy. After working for a year to update the finances and secure additional funding, while the current investors increasingly refused to continue the funding, and the attempt to pivot the technology from the legacy device to a completely new device, a biopsy needle. I was working 14 hour days and seeing the companys future looking bleaker. So, I resigned 12 months after I started.

Shortly after I left, the company was sold to another company and I do not know what became of the technology.

Career Reflection

 

How did you prepare for change?

I dont know if I ever prepared for change other than being open to it. Sometimes I resisted it, sometimes I avoided it, sometimes I ignored the need for it.

What ifs

Until I started writing this journal, I never considered what might have been. We tend to look back on things just as they were. However, many things could have gone differently. If I had not taken the job in Atlanta, who knows what that path might have been. Maybe NCR would have worked out. Moving to Europe. Had I not gone, I would never have had that experience that grew me. Taking the controller job with the trading company. Had I taken that job, I am retty sure it would have turned out badly. I had a bad feeling about the company and the leaders. Not going to SQL Financials. I could have gone to another company, but I wouldnt have had that termination experience. Not finding Caribiner. Caribiner was a great job. Not moving to NYCthat could have been great or an utter disaster. Not following up with McKesson. I could have bene afraid to follow up and never gotten the job that allowed me to really expand my financial situationfunding private school, cars, vacations, etc. Staying in the VP job for almost 7 years. Should have looked elsewhere then. Not seeing the writing on the wall after the President change. Should have looked elsewhere for sure then. But I was too comfortable to see it. Not suing McKesson for age and sex discrimination. So many what ifs/ The answers can never be known. You just have to take it one decision at a time.

How did priorities shift?

While enjoyed, and benefitted from my international experience tremendously after we returned from Europe, the kids were entering their growing up years. The Us economy was also entering a technology boom that drove the creation of new companies and dramatic changes in the financial regulations companies were subject to. I shift my focus to an IPO, which I did. But in hindsight, it was not the right choice. I was still relatively nave.

Once I left there, I joined a larger company that had good potential, but it didnt work out either. I was fortunate to find a role at McKesson that was both a huge company, but also technology and healthcare.

What was your most significant challenge?

I would point to several challenges in my career. The first was finding a job after not properly preparing through college and grad school. I was lucky to have been offered a job that allowed me to mature and grow. The next was getting agreement to move to Atlanta to start my international career. It almost didnt happen.. Ive had challenges with bosses, with other employees. I have experienced firing, corporate plans changing, political reorganizations, company sales, and personal resignation.

How did you adapt?

Through it all, while some of it was planning, other times it was riding by the seat of my pants.; but I just never gave up. I almost never let myself feel sorry for myself and mope. Even before I knew the phrase, If its to be, its up to me I was living it. Once I had a few successs, I gained confidence in myself that gave me confidence to face the next challenge.

What satisfaction did you find?

My satisfaction was what I eventually came to codify as what the best people lovechallenge, responsibility and recognition.. I made myself available, showed myself capable and were given as much challenge and responsibility as I wanted. The area where I have referred to in other places, is that I did not insist or ensure that I was being recognized as financially significantly as I should have been.

How did you plan career transitions?

I wish I could say my transition planning was always objective and calculating. It was not. But, there was some underlying direction. I suppose, it could be called a combination of planning and opportunism.

         Dad had helped me plot a strategy from high school t pursue international business. While I didnt think about it every day and there were times when it was an after thought, it eventually played out.

         I graduated from college without a plan and luckily someone called me.

         I did leave USC for NCR with a plan to get into business.

         While not planned, I left NCR for Lanier for an opportunity to get into International

         After about 8 years, I left Lanier to take on a CFO role with SQL Financials, a software company in a frenzied market.

         In transparency, I was fired from SQL Financials for a mistake that was my fault, but there were mitigating circumstances. The COO kept things close to his vest, and while he did tell me about the covenants in a conversation about a long list of covenants, he never gave me the document.

         After leaving SQL Financials, to restart my career, joining Caribiner was a calculated idea of joining a company in a smaller division with the idea that it could grow quite substantially. It appeared to be on track, and I was exceeding performance expectations. Unfortunately, leadership decisions took that off track. But I left deliberately with some time to find something else.

         For my next career restart, I joined McKesson because it was in technology and healthcare, a stable company and in Atlanta. It provided great stability for 11 years.

         I left McKesson when it was past time. I had climbed as far as I wanted to climb and was not going to be willing to move locations for another role. So, while I was ostensibly reorganized out, it was time. I should have been more proactive in looking.

         I left Given imaging when it was sold.

         I left Dune Medical when it was time.

How did you mentor others?

What wisdom did you share?

What achievements are you most proud of?

What would you do differently?

What advice would you give others?

What principles guided your leadership?

Over time, I developed two key mantras that guided my approach:

"If it's to be, It's up to me" - meaning that no matter what happens, I have to do whatever I can to make it happen.

"Run it so the best people love it" - understanding that the best people want challenge, freedom and recognition. Challenge of their capabilities, the freedom to make decisions that affect their work, and recognition in terms of acknowledgement and compensation.

How did your leadership evolve?

I eventually moved into CFO roles at medical device companies - one successful, one not successful. The contrast between these experiences taught me valuable lessons about what makes organizations work. Success isn't just about the numbers; it's about building the right team and culture.

[Theme Box: Leadership Insights]

  • Best people want three things:
    1. Challenge of their capabilities
    2. Freedom to make decisions
    3. Recognition (both acknowledgment and compensation)
  • Don't wait to be valued - know your worth
  • Build teams that can function without you
  • Success comes from enabling others

What career regrets do you have?

One of the most significant things about my career is I should have been more assertive about my value. I regret not recognizing I was better than I thought and not demanding what I was worth in the market. I was taught a work ethic of work as long as it takes to get the job done, and then others decide when it is time for you to get a raise. Part of that came from my upbringing of not rocking the boat. Part of it came from the way corporations indoctrinate you for their benefit to believe that you must pay your dues before being promoted or getting a raise, etc.

That is complete bullshit. People should be paid for the value they bring, from the very first moment. This is why I now believe that college athletes should be paid. They are the ones the fans are paying to see. It is not the coaches or the nice stadium. It is the performance of the players. Compensating them is going to raise other issues, but they should get paid for the value they bring.

On a similar subject, while it will be difficult to write a law that does not have unintended consequences, the people that work for corporation should receive more of the value created than they do. The idea generators and the CEOs are performing a portion of the task, but great ideas without the arms and legs to carry them out are worthless.

The old saying about "paying your dues" often masks a system designed to underpay talented people for as long as possible.

Workplace Reality

At McKesson, I witnessed and suffered age discrimination firsthand. Jeff Campbell, the CFO, once said in front of me (not realizing I was over 50), "We need to get rid of all these 50-year-olds and replace them with younger people." Shortly after that I was reorganized out. I probably should have sued for discrimination, which it clearly was. I briefly searched for an attorney and I found one that helped me negotiate some severance terms, but I also learned that few attorneys want to go after a big corporation. They want to work for them and make a living off them. Suing them on behalf of a former employee has less upside. First, they know they are going to be fighting a well-funded opponent, they may not have the skills to challenge them, and they cut off any chance of working for them in the future.

Who was your best boss

I have had a few bosses that were very helpful to me in my career.

Tom Campbell became one of my best bosses. He was in his 50's when I was in my late 20's. He had a long career at 3M, known for developing good management. Though his background was sales, anything outside that area that crossed his desk, he immediately copied me and included me. He promoted me to Finance Manager for Latin America and took me along on all his trips. The experience was invaluable and led to me later moving to Europe to handle the European division.

Who was your worst boss?

Ironically, my worst boss was the guy that hired me into the international position that kicked off my career in the first place. He was very controlling and did not like anyone doing anything outside of his control. When he saw that Tom Campbel, who was not yet my boss, had started including me and relying on me, he started to dislike me. One day he came into my office. He was about 40 something and I was about 27 or 28, so I had respect for his experience and position. He leaned over my desk, gritted his teeth and told me in his harshest growling voice that he was the Finance Manage for Latin America, and everything was to go through him. I had never been spoken to like that in a business setting. I didnt show it, but I was very taken back by that. He was known for becoming angry and speaking to people angrily. He was a good example of how not to lead.

Tom Campbell

I have had a couple of work-related people greatly influence my life. The first was Tom Campbell at Lanier. He threw me into every issue that he was ever involved in at Lanier and gave me exposure and opportunity to get involved in a wide range of topics. He supported me during some challenging political situations and generally gave me the confidence to take on more and grow.

Craig Niemiec

The other one I would mention is the CFO at McKesson that I worked for, Craig Niemiec. He taught me the concept of Run it so the best people love it. He was referring to how he managed. I came to see this as the best people want opportunity, challenge and recognition and reward. It is similar to what Tom Campbell did for me. He gave me responsibility for the work, freedom to deal with it my way, and recognition in terms of compensation and praise for good work.

Jerry Pierson

The two sayings, If its to be, its up to me. and Run it so the best people love it. helped guide my individual performance and managerial approach that lead to the successes that I had I business.

Which was your best job

It would be hard to pick my best job. I have had a lot of good jobs. My first job at the University of South Carolina was a great experience. I got to travel and use my language skills. My first Financial Analyst job was with NCR who was manufacturing the first multi-user personal computers in the world, and I was working on the finance issues surrounding it. My first job in international was great experience. Finance Manager for Latin America, Controller for Europe, Director of Finance for all of International. CFO for a startup software business. VP of Finance for Carabiner in NYC and multi-city creative company. VP of Finance at McKesson. then CFO. CFO for 2 medical device companies. One successful. one not successful.

Which was your worst job?

I had jobs that didnt work out, the way I envisioned them, but I learned a lot even from these. I cant really think of a worst job. E

Were you ever fired

I was fired twice. Once from the software company. I really wasnt a great fit for that job. I was fired or reorganized out of McKesson. That was internal politics. The guy that took over as president didnt like the fact that I had question his targets (they were too low) during planning season. So, when he was erroneously elevated to the job because he had been beating targets that had been set too low in the first place, he made it his mission to move me out, and eventually did. I know this because shortly after he was elevated to President of the division, the Corporate CFO called and asked if I could work with him, to which I said of course. But he was a prick from the very beginning.

What is your best advice for ones career?

Over the years, I took on two mantras so to speak.

The first is, If its to be, Its up to me. That just means, no matter what happens I have to do whatever I can to make it happen.

The second is, Run it so the best people love it. And the best people want challenge, freedom and recognition. Challenge of their capabilities, the freedom to make decisions that affect their work and recognition in terms of acknowledgement and compensation.

If there were a third one, it would be, Get paid what you are worth. The old ways of having you pay your dues is shorthand for stealing your time for little pay as long as they can get away with it.

What were your most important learnings?

Did you ever have trouble at work?

Did you ever quit a job?

Yes! And I recommend learning when it is appropriate to quit.

The Never-Ending Lawn

One of the first jobs I quit was one of my lawn cutting jobs. I still feel bad about it.

 

I went to the door of this house and asked the guy if he wanted his grass cut. He said, Sure, how much? I took a quick glance around and said, $5. He said, You want to take a look at the side. I said, No, thats okay, thinking How big could a side yard be? He said, Okay.

By then I had stopped dragging the lawn mower around the neighborhood and just went to get it if someone said yes. So, I ran home to get the mower. Still hadnt seen the side yard.

I liked to start by cutting all around the edges of the yard and work into the middle. So, I started in the front along the driveway and the sidewalk, across the front of the house to the side yard. I stopped and stared with my mouth open for several seconds. It was 3 or 4 times as large as the entire front. I had been so adamant about the price that I couldnt go ask to change it. I thought for a second, turned off the mower and went home. Really young, bad decision. I felt bad about it but was too inexperienced to have handled it differently. I assume they saw the lawnmower track and where it ended, figured out what had happened and probably laughed at my mistake.

The last job I quit, was the last job I held with Dune Medical. I did not quit because I could not do the job. I quit because the fate of the business was out of my control, and it was better for me to leave.

I do not like to quit. If I start something I want to finish it unless it becomes impossible.That is how I felt about the Dune Medical position.

What are your career regrets?

Looking back, I regret not recognizing I was better than I thought and demanding what I was worth in the market. The old ways of having you 'pay your dues' is shorthand for stealing your time for little pay as long as they can get away with it.

Family

 

Marriage

As I prepared to go on my internship. It was an assumption that if Kerri were to go with me, then we had to be married.

Springbrook

When we first moved to Columbia, SC, we rented an apartment on the southeastern side of town near Town Center Mall. It was a relatively easy commute to downtown because it was right off the main highway system. I think it was a duplex. It entered into a den, had a passthrough opening to a small kitchen, and had one bedroom upstairs. I made a mantle for over the fireplace and a coffee table with folding leaves.

Brady

We got Brady while living in the Springbrook apartment. He was the runt of the litter and turned out to be the best dog ever. But it almost ended in disaster within the first few days.

We had just gotten him and somehow he got lost. We looked everywhere in the neighborhood. Some of the local kids even helped. We were convinced someone had stolen him and were devastated. Then one of the kids came to us with him saying they found him.

I was never sure if they had found him or if he had been taken. But thankfully we got him back and he turned out to be the greatest.

He was so cute as a puppy and loved to chase a ball. He had long ears that would drag the ground as he sniffed along. He would get into the pond behind the house and come out filthy, but so happy. One day he found a crawdad and as he tried to sniff it, the crawdad would clamp onto his ears. Brady would shake his head to get it off. Sniff again and the process would start over.

Linbrook Drive, Columbia, South Carolina

We bought our first house in 1985 on Linbrook Dr. in Columbia, SC. William and Elizabeth were born while we lived in this house.

 

Parenthood

 

I have thousands of pictures and hours of video. I am so thankful for them because they allow me to relive our lives again from the point of view of perspective.

Each picture sends me back to that time and they actually invoke more emotion now than they did then.

When the person is not there, you don't know how it will be. As the father and the one most responsible for providing for them and setting a high standard for myself as to what I want that provision to be, I always felt more pressure. But once they were here, there was just no question if I were going to do whatever it took to provide for them.

What is it like being a father?

I cannot imagine a more fulfilling, satisfying, rewarding, joyful, uplifting experience than being a father. I simply cannot imagine my life without my children. I've told them this and will continue to tell them this - they have been and continue to be the great joy of my life. They have made my life. Nothing I ever did for work or sports or any other domain gave me more than them.

How did you figure out how to be a parent?

The question presume I learned. Im not sure I did. Figuring out how to be a parent is on-the-job-training. There are no manuals and you have to take it one experience at a time. I think I must have learned a lot by osmosis from my parents. They both loved each of us, and showed it. There was discipline, but also love. Everything seemed to revolve around us.

I saw it as my job and my purpose to provide for the family, and I wanted to provide the best I could and do the things my father and mother had done for me.

When I was in the 5th grade, the school had an orchestra come play for the students and then afterwards asked if any kids wanted to learn to play an instrument. For some reason I was attracted to the violin. When I got home I asked my mother if I could play the violin. She said I had to wait and ask my father when he got home. Of course, I didnt know what he was going to say and worried about it all afternoon. When he got home I asked him immediately, and without hesitation he said yes. It is one of the first instances I remember where we was focused on providing for me what I wanted or needed, even if I didn't see it exactly like that then.

Over the years, he always sought to do what was best for me. He let me decide to go to USC, but stepped into encourage me to transfer to Clemson when he saw I was not happy. He let us play sports, he provided vacations and experiences we would learn from. He gave us cars and everything we could need. He bought the lake house to be a place where we could get together.

Of course all of those experiences were impressing upon me how to be a parent when the time came.

Being a Father

Wow, its hard to reduce this to a short story. There is so much to it.

I guess first, Id say I cannot imagine a more fulfilling, satisfying, rewarding, joyful, uplifting experience.

I simply cannot imagine my life without them. Ive told them this and will continue to tell them this. They have been and continue to be the great joys of my life. They have made my life. Nothing I ever did for work or sports or any other domain gave me more than them. Even when I didnt know they were.

I have thousands of pictures and hours of video. I am so thankful for them, because they allow me to relieve from the point of view of perspective our lives again. Each picture sends me back to that time and they actually invoke more emotion now than they did then.

I was a reluctant father because, when the person is not there, I didnt know how it would be. As the father and the one most responsible for providing for it and setting a high standard for myself as to what I want that provision to be, I always felt more pressure.

But once they were here, there was just no question if I were going to do whatever it took to provide for them. I didnt go to work every day thinking about having to or need ting provide for them. I was already driven to do as well as I could. It was just a forgone conclusion that the back door was nailed shut. We are going forward. So much so that I never even considered the possibility of there being a back door.

William

Teaching William to drive

When William turned 15, he was eligible to get his learner's permit. I took him to the motor vehicle department to take his tests. He passed and got his permit. Thinking that he had already some practice driving, as we left, I attempted to give him the keys and let him drive. But apparently he had not practiced and did not want to drive. So, we drove to the Home Depot and let him drive slowly through the parking lot and practice parking.

Elizabeth

Teaching Elizabeth to Drive

When it was time for Elizabeth to learn to drive, I took her to a wide open parking lot that was part of a big trucking company. We switched seats and let her take the wheel. She was so afraid of what might happen that she would not take her foot off of the brake pedal. I finally convinced her to release the brake pedal very easily. As soon as the car started to move she would push the brake down hard. It was only after several starts and stops that she finally started letting the car rolled forward very slowly. Then she did not want to turn the wheel to make the car turn, even though the parking lot was completely empty. Eventually, I got her to begin driving in very big, very slow circles. But, she had had enough and wanted to go home.

Even though she started off very slowly, it was not long before she was completely comfortable behind the wheel and taking turns with speed and wearing the tires out on the sides. Now she is probably one of the most aggressive drivers I know.

Caroline

What's one thing you wish you'd known before becoming a parent?

How great it would be. How to be more allowing. I was strict. At times, too strict and it was not good for either of us.

What was it like becoming a parent?

Like a lot of things, I was not prepared to be a parent. Other than having parents myself, I had not specific teaching or discussions about parenthood. I had never even thought about it. Again, like a lot of things. As I write this, it seems to be a theme that I wish I had discovered sooner and I wish had endeavored to communicate to my children sooner. However, I think they were much better prepared simply because of the increase exposure that I had growing up and the opportunities they had growing up. But I still wish I had been more intentional about it.

Before I say this, I have to trust and know that my children know and live the love I have for them is infinite and I have and will give them anything I can help them. These comments are more a function of the concerns I had for my marriage, even from very early on. Frankly, I was not excited to learn my wife was going to have baby. I saw it as another hurdle to get over to get out of my marriage.

How did you prepare for parenthood?

Unfortunately, I didnt. I didnt know what I didnt know and ignored preparing for it.

What surprised you about being a parent?

What surprised me the most was the absolute dedication and love you have for this precious being.

What challenges did you face?

There were two types of challenges. The first was dealing with their crying. I distinctly remember standing in Williams room on Linbrook in the dark as he cried incessantly. At that moment I understood how young males could be pushed to violence against their children. I had a job, income, a family and social structure. Anyone who does not have that and experiences that, could more easily be pushed to violence. When we first moved to Atlanta and were living in an apartment, Elizabeth would cry every night. We would take turns rocking her or winding up her swing every three hours or so. Finally, we discovered it was an ear infection and we were able to resolve it. But neither of us knew hat to do.

Thankfully, I do not remember any ultimately serious challenges. I had a hard time getting William to learn responsibility, as I had done also. I think Elizabetha nd Caroline went through a few month period when they were areound 12 or 13 where they had issues with their mother, but I dont remember having any.

The most afraid I have ever been was during the eating disorder. I dont want to go into it because thankfully, it came to a perfect ending, but I was petrified for the possibilities of what could have happened.

What joys did you experience?

How did your priorities change?

What parenting style did you develop?

I am embarrassed to say now that I was too much of a disciplinarian, expecting young children to be far more perfect that they should be expected to be. I did what I knew, which was discipline from my parents, and I only learned to modify it later.

What activities did you do together?

Everyone seemed to be involved in some sporting activityfootball, cheerleading, basketball, baseball, swimming. Each of them had games or events very frequently. We went on road trips and

What values did you try to instill?

The ones that were instilled in me. Responsibility. Working hard. Doing your best.

What discipline approaches did you use?

How did you handle education decisions?

Early education decisions were fairly easy, but I didnt have the money to send them anywhere by public school. We chose where to live based on cost, but also on the school district.

The kids went to public schools for the neighborhoods we lived in until they were ready for high school. While we were in Belgium they went to St. Johns, which was a great beginning experience for both Willliam and Elizabeth.

The decision to send them to GACS was a combination of the state of the public high school. On a Friday night at the football game, most of the kids hung out behind the stands, they guys dressed like thugs and the girls dressed like child prostitutes; at the same time, a few of the kids on Williams travelling baseball team were already attending GACS, and having him join them there would be good for their sports team, and I had finally reached a financial point where I could afford it.. It was the best educational decision we made. The calibre of people they were able to socialize with was far better than what would have been available at the public school.

When it came to college, they each made their own choices. William chose Clemson over Georgia to play baseball. Caroline wanted to go to Clemson and earned a scholarship as a pole vaulter. And Elizabeth wanted to pursue her own trail in graphic arts are Auburn. All were out of state, but thankfully, we had the resources.

What was daily family life like?

How did you balance work and family?

From the very beginning, I remember hearing, they grow up fast. So, I made an effort to be at everything I could. I still probably could have done more, but I did not miss many events. I would arrive at baseball games and track meets in my coat and tie from work watching my little athletes play.

How did family relationships evolve?

What roles did extended family play?

Unlike the few times that I saw my grandparents, the kids saw their grandparents much more. We visited them and they visited us.

How did sibling relationships develop?

William was always the older, cool instigator. He picked on Elizbeth and Caroline. But with 5 years between Elizabeth and Caroline, Elizabeth took on Caroline as her own child, or at least a close friend. She set up school in the basement for her. Caroline had to earn early to stand up for herself and she did. When we sold the Edgewater house, I had to tighten the screws on the door hinges from all the slamming sit had taken when Caroline got fed up with being mistreated. To this day, she does not like to share her food with anyone because she was such and easy target for poaching fries or whatever else she had they wanted.

But one of the things, I love the most is how close they have stayed into their own families They took a trip to Chicago together, they make time for each other to have breakfast, lunch or dinner. They communicate with each other on every issue they are involved in. I am so happy to see all the cousins now playing together.

Homes & Moves

How did you choose locations?

We chose Linbrook because it was a good location for me to commute to work and we got a good price. We chose Amberglen, which was well outside the perimeter in Atlanta, because it was the only thing we could find that we wanted and we could afford. We chose Edgewater because it was a good neighborhood in the Collins Hill district where we thought the kids would go to high school. We chose Andelle because it was a nicer home, closer to my office, near Alpharetta, the kids were driving to school.

What neighborhoods shaped family life?

Our Meadow Trace neighborhood likely had the most influence on the family we had several friend families in the neighborhood. We would have cul-de-sac parties and do things together with the kids and adults.

What home improvements did you make?

Unfortunately not enough. Ne of my regrets is that I did not make enough improvements in each of the houses we owned. I should have improved the yard at Amberglen, I should have finished the basement at Woodbrook and the basement at Andelle.

Home improvements are always and investment with unknown payoff, except for the enjoyment that you will get out of them. The Edgewater house especially would have been a great investment in the basement because the kids were in the hear of their school years.

My advice is to make the investment

What moves were significant?

Moving to Atlanta was the most significant, it launched my international career. Moving to Europe was a great experience for all in the family.

How did locations affect lifestyle?

Living in Belgium was certainly a change in lifestyle. Clothing was different, food was different.

What made each place special?

Linbrook was our first home where William and Elizabeth were born and where we got Brady.

Woodbrook was where the kids developed great friendships and experiences.

Edgewater was where the kids became adults.

How did you contribute?

In Belgium I helped coach the soccer team, never having played soccer before. I coached Elizabeths basketball team, when basketball had never been my thing.

What principles guided you?

How did your outlook mature?

This is a deep question, and there is a time where I would not have even had an idea of how to answer it. I have always bee in a hurry to get somewhere. Whatever it was that I was trying toa chieve, I was tying to achieve it better and faster. That is why I worked long hours. I always though that I would somehow either catch up or get ahead. I never really challenged this perspective. Until recently. I look back at all the work that I did for each of the companies. All of the closing and presentations, and processes and stuff that I did, unfortunately is not even remotely remembered. Nor should it be.

I never saw business as simply a game within the larger game of life that you just play to provide income and wealth. There is not right or wrong, there is only functional or nonfunctional.

There is a place for hard work and perfection is, but not at the expense of the experience.

The Future

 

Current Focus

I want to continue growing in business and building financial security while spending quality time with my children, their families, and supporting their interests.
Current Focus

Looking Forward

I believe I am viewed as mature, successful and capable, but that has not always been the case. I want anyone reading this and appreciate my life as one they can understand and empathize with. The challenges they face now, that seem so life-altering to them, I experienced too. Despite uncertainties, I managed to build a successful life.

I do have a dream I have not achieved yet, but I am keeping it to myself. And I will keep on trying.

Personal Reflection

 

What advice would you give your younger self?

10 - Dont worry about growing up too fast. Work on educating yourself, making good friends, enjoying the experiences presented to you but dont be in too much of a hurry to be a teenager.

Teenager - This is a tough period. You start knowing more outside yourself and your family and friends and you begin to believe you know more than you do. You question and doubt the wisdom of those that came before you. Explore new ideas, but do not be so quick to throw out older ideas. They tend to be older ideas because they are good ideas.

18 - Get to know yourself. Love yourself for who you are. Get comfortable with yourself and who you are. Be happy and proud of who you are. Recognize how good you really are and be satisfied with that. Everyone else is going through the same thoughts to some degree. Don't judge yourself too harshly.

21 - When you get out of college, those offering you jobs do not expect you to be perfect and know everything. What they look for most is interpersonal skills and a basic understanding of the field you are entering. They will need to teach you the details of any job you take. Don't look at everything as a judgment of your value that you have to protect. You can make mistakes. Take your job seriously, but don't take yourself so seriously and mix in fun with the hard work.

25 - You can only love another when you love yourself. If a relationship does not satisfy you, do not be afraid to get out of it and find one that suits you. Life is tough enough without having to spend a lot of it with the wrong person.

As a new parent - Be amazed at the miracle of life. Enjoy every degree of their development. Don't try to be perfect nor try to have them be perfect. Having kids is messy and not a straight line. Loosen up and go with the flow.

40

50

         Moments of self-discovery, personal milestones, and evolving perspectives.

         How technological advancements have influenced your career and life.

What were the most significant challenges you faced and the lessons learned?

 

What did you wear when younger that mortifies you now?

When I was a teenager, it was the 70s and hairstyles were very long. Also, I was very shy and I grew my hair out so long that it flipped up above my eyes and curled up in the back. When I got a haircut I didnt want them to cut anything and if they cut what I thought was too much, I felt very self-conscious.

What advice would you give your great grandchildren?

Thats a wide-open question.

Keep your body fit, it is the vehicle you travel through life in.

Keep your mind fit, it is the foundation of the reality you experience.

Build relationships with everyone above work or play.

Be comfortable with yourself and find a mate that you know in your heart you can be happy with and will be happy with you.

Prepare yourself so you can provide value to others and expect to be compensated for the value you bring.

Balance savings and spending. In the beginning you may not be earning a lot, but over time that will grow. You will save some for the future but be sure to spend enough so that you don't regret not doing some of the things you wish you had done. I spent a lot and saved a lot. There were times when I could have sent money to do things to the house or other larger expenditures that I put off not believing I could afford it. Later, when more income came, it turned out that I could have redone those basements and enjoyed them when the kids were there to enjoy them.

What is your best advice when it comes to work?

When I was in it, I never knew if what I was doing was going to be enough. Enough to get that next promotion and increase in compensation. I didnt have anyone that could look and see that I was doing more than enough, nor anyone to advise that I should be being compensated more than I was for what I was doing. Its impossible to know if I had worked less would I have had the same opportunities and success, and I would rather have done a little bit too much than a little bit too little.

So do the best job you know how to do, but you will never accomplish it all. There will always be more work, there is no catching up to get very much ahead. It is better to just pace yourself through the work and get accomplished what there is time to accomplish.

No one is indispensable, even you. If you leave, someone else will fill the void. Differently than you, but it will be filled.

Youll never get ahead.

Work is supposed to supplement life, not become it.

Incorporate it into your life as that which provides the income, but do not let it become your life. There is more satisfaction in children and family than work and accolades.

When youve worked at a t a company 15 years, youll get to pick out a cheap trinket as your recognition.

Beliefs

What personal expectations do you hold yourself to?

I believe that I hold myself to high standards. That was not always the case. My dad used to seemingly constantly be reminding us of what he called, The Big Rresponsibility. I hated it at the time, but, of course, in hindsight, he was right to have done it, and I am far better for it. I have not always lived up to my high standards, but I continually try.

Whats your idea of happiness?

This is a tough one for me because I believe I have struggled with this my whole life, and I am not sure why. I think happiness comes from accepting the past for what it is, understanding that life and people are imperfect, and will never be perfect. But being confident in the person you are and accepting of people as they are and confident that whatever happens, you will be okay.

That is sometimes harder to do than others. There will be situations that do not meet your expectations and be very negative for you. It is not easy to do this, but you just have to let go of that expectation and find something else that is satisfying.

Who/What inspires you?

I am not sure I would called it inspired; perhaps awed, but the earth and life and the universe are awe inspiring. The beauty in the chaotic interaction of animate and inanimate objects.

What drives me is a sense of curiosity. I recently discovered the enneagram, and was surprised to find out that I seek to understand. That captures me very well. I want to understand how everything works. Partially this is because I learned from my father that I want to make everything work better. To do that you have to know how it works.

What beliefs do you have that are different than your parents?

I have come to believe more like my parents as I have found that their beliefs better serve my life.

I remember a time in college. I was a business major and thought I knew something about business. My father owned a business and was considering giving stock in the company to the employees. I felt he was giving too much. But I have come to believe that no matter how genius the founder or the leader of the business, without those to execute what needs to be executed to manifest success, that idea or leadership is not worth very much. I dont know how it could be expressed in legislation, and I am for capitalism, not socialism, but a greater portion of the value created by the employees should be shared with the employees. It would be best if this were seen by the leadership of the business that this is in the best long term best interest of the business. But, if they dont, while I am not exactly what the form should be, it should be required.

What have you changed your mind about over the years?

THEMES

 

Financial

 

What are your views on money?

My memories and thoughts on money have changed a lot over time.

Early Memories of Money

While we never discussed it that I recall, I think money was tight in the beginning.

I didnt know were renting at the time, but I found out later that we were renting the houses that we lived in at first. The first one and Greenhill Rd were rented.

MoneyTough times.

When I was very young, I did not know much about how much money we did or didn't have. It was just normal. I dont remember not having something.

I do remember that we would all go to a store on the weekend sometimes, that for me was a very long way across town. It was called the Cash and Carry or Kash and Karry. Later I found out we went there to buy groceries inexpensively and Mom and Dad would buy enough to last a long time. We mostly ate at home. Not much eating out. A big treat for us was to go to Howard Johnson's (an old hotel chain that doesn't exist anymore.) My favorite was fried clams! I remember that my mother used to cook meatloaf, salmon croquettes, mashed potatoes, macaroni and cheese. Like all kids, we would ask for things all the time. There were times that we would ask for something and to keep us from asking, she would say she didn't have any money. Not knowing any better, we would say, "That's okay, just write a check!"

Kash and Karry

Although I never felt like we were poor, I think money was not always plentiful When we lived in Taylors, Mom and Dad would drive to Greenville to a store called Kash and Karry. I suppose the food was less expensive. They would leave us in the car (before the threats we have today) and come out with loads of bags and fill up the station ago with groceries.

 

First Purchased House

The house on Woodbine Terrace was the first one that Mom and Dad bought. I think Mom told me they paid either $15,000 for the Creighton Street house. Either way, in todays terms, that is 150,000.

Kash and Karry to Save Money

Mom and Dad also used to go to Kash and Karry as I mention in another story. They drove quite a ways across townat least 30 minutest go to a cheaper discount store.

Just Write a Check

Of course, as kids, we were always asking for things. Mom would say, I dont have any money for that. After a while, we saw how she operated, got wise to her and would say, Just write a check! of course not understanding the full concept. But money certainly didnt grow on trees.

Odd Jobs

I dont remember it as having to work for money. It was just something that I wanted to do. Perhaps because I wasn;t being given money by my parents. But, as I tell in other stories, I used to collect bottles off the side of the road every so often. I would cut grass and I even tried selling Christmas cards.

The Business Grew

But, apparently Dads business was growing and the money issues were fewer. In 1975 we moved to a new house (new to us). Dad built the first of his office and manufacturing buildings off Airport Rd. And Christmas one year included motorcycles. Dad bought himself a new car, and in high school he bought me a car. And he gave me a gas allowance of $5 a week. I cant remember the exact timing, but there was stage where I was supposed to be getting 75 cents a week allowance, but it wasnt very regular and I dont remember missing it. So I must have been getting what I needed or wanted.

We Moved Again

Also, as my brothers were leaving middle school and going to high school, we moved again into an even bigger house. We took vacations to Florida and out West.

First Semester College Money

While I had the things I needed, I was not getting the money education that perhaps I should have. The first semester in college, Dad gave me $5,000 for the semester. From this I was to pay for tuition, board, books, food, clothes everything. I suppose it was his way of teaching me to manage money. But, I did not do a good job. I had to go to the bak and figure out how to get a checking account. Credit cards for students were not common.) In hindsight, it was a lot of money. I went out I bought clothes. I had gas money. I was writing checks for pretty much everything I wanted. My roommates clearly did not have as much as I had access to.

$5,000 in 1975 is equivalent to about $25,000 today. That may seem about right, but tuition and board have gone up tremendously. My tuition and board was probably under $1,000. The rest was basically spending money. As the semester neared the end, I realized I was not going to have enough money to get to the next semester. I stopped buying clothes and food out. Started eating apples everyday and even sold my blood for $20 to help get me through. I managed to make it, but I had literally blown through all that money. It was my fault, and I felt bad about it, but when I told Dad, he felt bad that I had not told him and had eaten apples and sold my blood. I think he gave me the same amount the next semester, but I was much better about managing it from then on.

While it was never spoken of, things just got better.
